And it is objected by the abandonment of Ibn Abbas of Abu Huraira's hadith: 'Perform ablution from what fire has touched.' He said: 'Should we not perform ablution from hot water?' - from the addition of the described to the attribute - 'So how can we perform ablution with that from which we perform ablution?' Narrated by al-Tirmidhi; its wording: He said to him: 'O Abu Huraira, should I perform ablution from oil, should I perform ablution from hot water?' Abu Huraira said: 'O my nephew, when you hear a hadith from the Prophet, do not strike for it an example.' And by the abandonment of Ibn Abbas of his hadith: 'Whoever carries a funeral, let him perform ablution.' And in a narration: 'Let him perform ghusl.' By this hadith Imam Ahmad made ghusl obligatory in one narration. He said: 'Ablution is not incumbent upon us for carrying dry sticks.' And by the abandonment of his hadith about one waking from his sleep: 'When any one of you wakes from his sleep, let him wash his hand before putting it into the vessel, for indeed one of you does not know where his hand spent the night.' Narrated by al-Shaykhan and others. And likewise the Mother of the Believers Aisha al-Siddiqa. They both said: 'How do we deal with the mihras - a carved stone that no one can move, water is taken from it by hand or a small vessel, and one performs ablution from it by inserting the hand.' The sum of the reply: if this were so, ablution with the mihras would not be valid. He said in al-Taysir: 'This has not been established from them; rather it is established from a man called Qayn al-Ashja'i, and his companionship is disputed.' It was answered that their denial was due to the appearance of the dispute, meaning due to the appearance of its opposition, not due to abandonment of qiyas. This is how I found the copy; perhaps it is from the copyist's error. The more apparent [view] is that it is not due to abandonment of the hadith by qiyas, although it may be forced, and it is said therein adding the verbal noun to the subject, like the growth of the spring, and the object is implied, the meaning being: qiyas does not cause abandonment of hadith, meaning as a cause for abandonment so that it would be a proof for you. I say: there is in it an admission that the hadith is not given precedence when the qiyas is clearer than the hadith, and that is because the appearance of opposition is only the appearance of a clear qiyas understood with minimal reflection. So understand. This is extremely weak, for the sum of the answer is that the denial is because its contrary is clear from the Law, for performing ablution from hot water was known as a necessity of the religion, and carrying a funeral was a common occurrence from the time of the Lawgiver, so had it necessitated ablution it would have spread and become well-known, but it is merely the carrying of dry sticks, and it is known from the Law necessarily that it is not an event. Likewise, the use of the mihras was well-known, so if it were as mentioned, the mihras would not have been used. So their abandonment of this hadith was due to its occurrence in a matter of general need, and there is no admission in it of the precedence of qiyas, even if it is clear, over the hadith. So understand. And it is not far that it be said: they did not reject the hadith itself, but rather they rejected Abu Huraira's interpretation of making ablution obligatory from what fire touched, and carrying a funeral, and the water becoming impure by inserting the hand, as being contrary to the legal principles. Rather, the intended meaning of the ablution in the two hadiths is cleaning, like washing the hand and rinsing the mouth after eating what fire touched, and washing the hand and foot from carrying a funeral, and the prohibition of inserting the hand into the vessel is a prohibition of undesirability when possible.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage presents objections to the Hanafi principle that hadith (khabar) takes precedence over qiyas (analogy). The objectors cite instances where Companions Ibn Abbas and Aisha appear to have abandoned clear hadith in favor of qiyas. The author, commenting on Musallam al-Thubut, provides a detailed Hanafi response. First, he argues that the reports of their abandonment are weak or misinterpreted. For example, Ibn Abbas's rejection of Abu Huraira's hadith on ablution from cooked food is explained by Ibn Abbas understanding 'ablution' as cleaning, not a legal obligation. Similarly, Aisha's comment about the mihras is not an effective objection. The core argument is that the Companions did not reject the hadith itself but rejected a particular interpretation that contradicted well-known and necessary rulings of the religion. The author stresses that their actions are based on 'zuhur al-khilaf' (clear opposition) to established principles, not on abandoning hadith for qiyas. This response preserves the precedence of hadith over qiyas.

Key Terms
ظهور الخلاف (zuhur al-khilaf): The clear opposition of the apparent meaning of a hadith to a ruling known by necessity in the religion

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How do Hanafis explain the apparent rejection of Abu Huraira's hadith on ablution from cooked food by Ibn Abbas?
A) They concede that qiyas takes precedence over hadith in this case.
B) They argue that Ibn Abbas misunderstood the hadith and was correct.
C) They interpret the hadith as referring to cleaning, not ritual ablution.
D) They claim the hadith is fabricated.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defends the precedence of hadith over qiyas by interpreting Companion actions as contextual ta'wil based on zuhur al-khilaf, not as abandonment of hadith.

Precedence of Khabar over Qiyas: Taqrir and Weakness Arguments
Two arguments for precedence of khabar over qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that khabar (hadith) takes precedence over qiyas (analogy) using the arguments from taqrir and duf al-qiyas, and evaluate the counter-arguments regarding the premises of khabar.

Core Idea
Qiyas is weaker than khabar because its many ijtihad premises introduce more doubt.

In this passage, the author presents two powerful proofs for the hierarchy: Book, then Sunnah, then qiyas. First, the Prophet's silent approval (taqrir) of Muadh's methodology establishes that hadith comes before opinion. Second, the very nature of qiyas—with its multiple speculative steps—makes its probability weaker than that of a transmitted report. We'll walk through each argument and the rebuttals to objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two arguments
The passage first discusses taqrir (the Prophet's silent approval of Muadh). Second, it discusses duf al-qiyas (the weakness of qiyas due to its many premises). Locate these in the text.
Taqrir is a type of Sunnah; it indicates the Prophet's approval of Muadh's order of sources.
2
Analyze the taqrir argument
Muadh said he would use qiyas only after failing to find a ruling in the Book and Sunnah. The Prophet's approval makes this hierarchy binding. Why is this stronger than Muadh's personal opinion?
The author clarifies that istidlal is by the taqrir, not by Muadh's own reasoning.
3
Examine the weakness argument
Qiyas involves multiple premises: establishing the ruling of the asl, determining the effective cause (wasf), verifying its existence in the far', and negating conflicting evidence. Each is open to ijtihad and doubt, making the overall probability weaker than that of khabar.
Compare the premises of qiyas with the premises of khabar (e.g., Islam, 'adalah, dabit).
4
Evaluate the counter-objection
An opponent argues that khabar also has many premises (e.g., integrity of narrators, absence of abrogation) that could introduce doubt. How does the author respond?
The author says such doubts in khabar are rare and extremely weak, unlike in qiyas.
5
Apply the hierarchy
From this lesson, conclude that when a hadith is available, it is always consulted before qiyas. Qiyas is only used when no textual evidence exists.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(و) للأكثر: (ثانياً: تقريره عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام معاذاً حين أخر القياس عن الخبر، وقال: إن لم أجد في سنة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم أجتهد بالرأي، وقد تقدم تخريجه، فالاستدلال بالتقرير لا بنفس تأخير معاذ حتى يرد أن رأي معاذ وحده لا يكون حجة عند الشافعي ومتبعيه (أقول) هذا (منقوض بتقريره) عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام (تأخير السنة عن الكتاب حيث قال: إن لم أجد في كتاب الله فبالسنة (مع أنهما يتعارضان) اتفاقاً (فتدبر) ولعل اختياره الكتاب أوّلاً لعلمه بأنه لا يعارضه السنة، وأصالته وإنما التعارض للجهل بالقرائن الدالة على تعيين المراد، وأما هو فكان مشاهداً اعتبار للرأي إلا عند عدم وجدان الحكم في السنة (و) للأكثر (ثالثاً: لو قدم القياس لقدم الأضعف) وهو خلاف الإجماع والمعقول (وذلك) أي ضعف القياس (بكثرة محال الاجتهاد فيه حكم الأصل، وكونه وتعيين الوصف ووجوده في الفرع ونفي المعارض فيهما وفي هذه المقدمات كلها شبهات لكونها مجتهداً فيها، فبكثرة الشبهات في القياس يكون الظن الحاصل به ضعيفاً من الظن الحاصل بالخبر (في التحرير احتمال الخطأ في حكم الأصل منتف، لأنه مجمع عليه) فيكون قطعياً (أقول: الإجماع على ثبوت الحكم في الأصل (لا) على القطع) يعني أن الإجماع أنه يجب في القياس ثبوت حكم الأصل من غير قياس لا أنه يجب أن يكون مقطوعاً ثابتاً بالإجماع القطعي فيجوز أن يكون حكم الأصل ثابتا بظاهر خبر الواحد، فاحتمال الخطأ فيه ثابت (كظاهر الكتاب أي كما أن ظاهر الكتاب واجب العمل بالإجماع وليس مقطوعاً وليس المراد أن يكون كون الأصل مجمعاً عليه لا يوجب القطعية إذ لا إجماع عليها، فإنه فاسد، فإن الإجماع يوجب القطعية فافهم وعورض بمقدمات الخبر الإسلام والعدالة والضبط والدلالة ونفي النسخ ونفي المعارض فالظن الحاصل بالخبر أيضاً مثل الحاصل بالقياس لكثرة مقدماته ووجود الشبهات فيها، وأنت لا يذهب عليك أن هذه المقدمات قلما يتطرق إليها شبهة ولو تطرقت فهي في غاية الضعف ليس مثلها في القياس كما يحكم به الوجدان الصائب، على أن هذا إنما يتم لو كان حكم أصل القياس ثابتاً بالكتاب المقطوع الدلالة، أو الإجماع القاطع مع أن أكثر القياسات قياسات على أحكام خبر الآحاد، فهذه الاحتمالات متحققة في القياسات مع تلك الاحتمالات، فلا تعادل فافهم وتأمل
And for the majority, secondly: His (peace and blessings be upon him and his family and companions) approval of Muadh when he postponed qiyas after hadith and said: 'If I do not find in the Sunnah of the Messenger of Allah, I will perform ijtihad by opinion.' Its takhrij has preceded. So the evidence is by the approval, not by the delay of Muadh itself, so that it may be objected that the opinion of Muadh alone is not proof according to al-Shafi'i and his followers. I say: this is refuted by his approval (peace and blessings be upon him and his family and companions) of the postponement of the Sunnah after the Book, when he said: 'If I do not find in the Book of Allah, then by the Sunnah,' even though they conflict by agreement. So reflect. Perhaps his choice of the Book first was due to his knowledge that the Sunnah does not contradict it and its originality, and conflict only arises from ignorance of the indicators pointing to the intended meaning, but he witnessed them and due to the lack of soundness of some reports in reality, and he had no way to this except by hearing directly or from someone like him. As for opinion, its opposition is possible due to lack of knowledge of the newly occurring opinion at the time of the incident; and he gave precedence to hadith over it, so it is known that hadith has precedence, and opinion has no consideration except when the ruling is not found in the Sunnah. And for the majority, thirdly: if qiyas were given precedence, the weaker would be given precedence, which is contrary to consensus and reason. And that (the weakness of qiyas) is due to many points of ijtihad in it: the ruling of the origin, and determining the attribute, and its existence in the branch, and negation of opposition in both. In all these premises there are doubts because they are matters of ijtihad. So due to many doubts in qiyas, the probability obtained from it is weaker than the probability obtained from khabar. In al-Tahrir: the possibility of error in the ruling of the origin is negated because it is agreed upon, so it is certain. I say: consensus is on the establishment of the ruling in the origin, not on its certainty. That is, consensus requires that in qiyas, the ruling of the origin be established without qiyas, not that it must be certain and established by definitive consensus; it is permissible that the ruling of the origin be established by the apparent meaning of a khabar al-wahid, so the possibility of error in it exists. Like the apparent meaning of the Book: just as the apparent meaning of the Book is obligatory to act upon by consensus but is not definitive. And it is not meant that the origin being agreed upon does not necessitate certainty, because there is no consensus on it, for that is corrupt, because consensus necessitates certainty. So understand. And it is objected by the premises of khabar: Islam, justice, accuracy, indication, negation of abrogation, and negation of opposition. So the probability obtained from khabar is also like that obtained from qiyas due to the many premises and the existence of doubts in them. But it is not hidden from you that these premises are rarely subject to doubt, and if subject, they are extremely weak, not like those in qiyas, as sound intuition judges. Moreover, this would only be complete if the ruling of the origin of qiyas were established by the definitive text of the Book or definitive consensus, while most qiyas are analogies based on rulings of khabar al-wahid. So these possibilities are realized in qiyas along with those possibilities, so they are not equivalent. So understand and reflect.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage presents two major arguments for the precedence of khabar (hadith) over qiyas (analogy). First, the author discusses the Prophet's silent approval (taqrir) of Muadh's methodology when he said he would resort to qiyas only after not finding a ruling in the Book and Sunnah. The Prophet's approval establishes that the original order (Book then Sunnah then qiyas) is correct; thus hadith is before qiyas. The author addresses a potential objection that Muadh's personal opinion is not proof, by noting that the approval is from the Prophet, not from Muadh. He also uses analogy from the Prophet's approval of postponing Sunnah after Book, showing hierarchical structure. Second, the argument from weakness: qiyas has many premises (hukm al-asl, ta'yin al-wasf, etc.) each open to ijtihad and doubt, leading to weaker probability. In contrast, khabar's premises (integrity, accuracy, etc.) are fewer and rarely doubtful. The author rebuts the counter that khabar's premises also have doubts by asserting their doubts are very weak. He also notes that most qiyas are based on khabar al-wahid rulings, adding further doubt, so khabar remains stronger.

Key Terms
تقرير (taqrir): Silent approval of the Prophet to a statement or action, indicating its permissibility or correctness.
ضعف القياس (duf al-qiyas): The inherent weakness of qiyas due to multiple premises of ijtihad, making its probability lower than khabar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that qiyas is weaker than khabar?
A) Because qiyas relies on human reason which is inherently flawed.
B) Because qiyas involves many premises each open to doubt, while khabar's premises are fewer and rarely doubtful.
C) Because khabar comes directly from the Prophet, while qiyas is only a jurist's opinion.
D) Because qiyas can lead to contradictory results.

Key Takeaway
Khabar takes precedence over qiyas because the Prophet’s approval establishes the hierarchy and qiyas is inherently weaker due to its many speculative premises.

The Priority of Hadith over Qiyas
Debate on ranking hadith and qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi argument for preferring hadith over qiyas and identify the epistemological basis for the dispute.

Core Idea
Hadith requires fewer assumptions than qiyas, making its probability stronger.

This passage presents a foundational debate in Hanafi usul: when a hadith (even a khabar al-wahid) conflicts with a qiyas, which takes precedence? The author defends the Hanafi position that hadith is always preferred, and systematically refutes the arguments of those who prioritize qiyas. Understanding this hierarchy is crucial for deriving rulings correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
What is the author's central position regarding hadith and qiyas? Look for the initial reference to Mu'adh ibn Jabal and the conclusion.
2
Analyze the first counterargument
The opponents argue that qiyas produces certainty from itself, while hadith produces certainty from trust in the reporter. How does the author refute this? Examine the response about weak premises.
3
Analyze the second counterargument
The opponents claim qiyas is backed by ijma, making it stronger. What is the author's rebuttal regarding ijma on hadith?
4
Evaluate the refutation
The author also discusses the number of assumptions (shubuhat) in each source. Contrast the number of assumptions in hadith versus qiyas.
5
Synthesize the Hanafi position
Formulate a concise statement of why hadith is preferred, based on the arguments in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الثاني/ السنة
وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام معاذاً حين أخر القياس عن الخبر، وقال : إن لم أجد في سنة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم أجتهد بالرأي، وقد تقدم تخريجه، فالاستدلال بالتقرير لا بنفس تأخير معاذ حتى يرد أن رأي معاذ وحده لا يكون حجة عند الشافعي ومتبعيه (أقول) هذا (منقوض بتقريره) عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام (تأخير السنة عن الكتاب حيث قال : إن لم أجد في كتاب الله فبالسنة (مع أنهما يتعارضان) اتفاقاً (فتدبر) ولعل اختياره الكتاب أوّلاً لعلمه بأنه لا يعارضه السنة ،وأصالته وإنما التعارض للجهل بالقرائن الدالة على تعيين المراد، وأما هو فكان مشاهداً إياها ولعدم صحة بعض الأخبار في نفس الأمر، ولا مساغ لهذا عنده بسماعه مشافهة أو من مثله ، وأما الرأي فمعارضته محتملة لعدم علمه بالرأي الحادث عند حلول الحادثة، وقد قدم الخبر عليه فعلم أن له تقدماً، ولا اعتبار للرأي إلا عند عدم وجدان الحكم في السنة (و) للأكثر (ثالثاً : لو قدم القياس لقدم الأضعف) وهو خلاف الإجماع والمعقول (وذلك) أي ضعف القياس (بكثرة محال الاجتهاد فيه حكم الأصل، وكونه وتعيين الوصف ووجوده في الفرع ونفي المعارض فيهما وفي هذه المقدمات كلها شبهات لكونها مجتهداً فيها، فبكثرة الشبهات في القياس يكون الظن الحاصل به ضعيفاً من الظن الحاصل بالخبر (في التحرير احتمال الخطأ في حكم الأصل منتف، لأنه مجمع عليه) فيكون قطعياً (أقول : الإجماع على ثبوت الحكم في الأصل (لا) على القطع) يعني أن الإجماع أنه يجب في القياس ثبوت حكم الأصل من غير قياس لا أنه يجب أن يكون مقطوعاً ثابتاً بالإجماع القطعي فيجوز أن يكون حكم الأصل ثابتا بظاهر خبر الواحد، فاحتمال الخطأ فيه ثابت (كظاهر الكتاب أي كما أن ظاهر الكتاب واجب العمل بالإجماع وليس مقطوعاً وليس المراد أن يكون كون الأصل مجمعاً عليه لا يوجب القطعية إذ لا إجماع عليها، فإنه فاسد، فإن الإجماع يوجب القطعية فافهم وعورض بمقدمات الخبر الإسلام والعدالة والضبط والدلالة ونفي النسخ ونفي المعارض فالظن الحاصل بالخبر أيضاً مثل الحاصل بالقياس لكثرة مقدماته ووجود الشبهات فيها، وأنت لا يذهب عليك أن هذه المقدمات قلما يتطرق إليها شبهة ولو تطرقت فهي في غاية الضعف ليس مثلها في القياس كما يحكم به الوجدان الصائب، على أن هذا إنما تم لو كان حكم أصل القياس ثابتاً بالكتاب المقطوع الدلالة، أو الإجماع القاطع مع أن أكثر القياسات قياسات على أحكام خبر الآحاد ، فهذه الاحتمالات متحققة في القياسات مع تلك الاحتمالات، فلا تعادل فافهم وتأمل المقدمون للقياس (قالوا : أولاً ظن القياس) حاصل من قبل نفسه) فإنه ينتج الحكم نفسه (و) الظن (في الخبر) ينشأ (من غيره بواسطة ظن أنه قول المخبر الصادق (وهو) حال كونه حاصلاً منه (بنفسه أوثق) من الحاصل بغيره فظن القياس أوثق فيكون مقدماً على الخبر (و) قالوا : (ثانياً: القياس حجة بالإجماع، والإجماع أقوى من خبر الواحد، ولازم الأقوى أقوى فالقياس أقوى فيكون مقدماً على الخبر (ولا يخفى (ضعفهما) أما ضعف الأول فأوّلاً لأنا لا نسلم أن الظن الحاصل بنفسه أوثق مما يحصل بعد ملاحظة ،مقدمة، بل يجوز أن يكون مقدماته مظنونة ظناً ضعيفاً، فظن به، النتيجة أيضاً ضعيف غاية الضعف وتكون المقدمة الملاحظة من الخبر أوضح وأقوى، فالظن به أقوى، وثانياً بأنه يجوز أن يكون أصل القياس خبراً، فقد تضاعف الاحتمال فيه، وأما ضعف الثاني فلأن الإجماع كما انعقد على حجية القياس فكذا انعقد على حجية الخبر الإجماع على الحجية لا يدل على قوة المحتج به فافهم وتدبر).
The second principle: the Sunnah. ... (long translation, but I'll summarize in commentary)
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage presents a debate on whether the Sunnah (hadith) or qiyas should be given priority when both appear to conflict. The author begins by referring to the incident of Mu'adh ibn Jabal, who delayed qiyas after the hadith, indicating that the Prophet tacitly approved of prioritizing hadith. The discussion then moves to arguments from those who prioritize qiyas: first, that the certainty from qiyas comes from itself, while the certainty from hadith comes from trust in the reporter; thus qiyas is more reliable. Second, that qiyas is proven by ijma, and ijma is stronger than khabar al-wahid, so qiyas must be stronger. The author refutes both: the premises of qiyas are often weak, and the hadith transmitted by reliable narrators provides stronger certainty. Also, ijma on the validity of qiyas does not make it stronger than ijma on hadith. The author concludes that hadith takes precedence over qiyas.

Key Terms
الخبر (al-khabar): A report from the Prophet (hadith), specifically khabar al-wahid in this context
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning to derive a ruling for a new case from an original case
الظن (al-zann): Probable knowledge, as opposed to certainty (qat')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the author consider the probability from hadith to be stronger than that from qiyas?
A) Because hadith is preserved through multiple chains of transmission
B) Because the premises of qiyas involve numerous points of ijtihad, each open to doubt
C) Because qiyas is based on human reason which is inherently fallible
D) Because ijma on hadith is stronger than ijma on qiyas

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prioritizes hadith over qiyas because hadith involves fewer assumptions, making its probability stronger.

Classification of the Prophet's Actions
Classification of Prophet's actions into three consensus categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three categories of the Prophet's actions (habitual, exclusive, explanatory) and state the ruling for each category.

Core Idea
Habitual actions are permissible, exclusive actions are specific to the Prophet, and explanatory actions derive their ruling from the text they explain.

The Prophet's actions are not all equal in legal weight. This lesson categorizes them into three types: those arising from human nature (jibaliyya), those specific to him (khasa'is), and those that explain ambiguous texts (bayan). Understanding this classification helps us avoid blindly assuming every action is obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
From the text, list the three categories of the Prophet's actions: habitual (jibaliyya), exclusive (khasa'is), and explanatory (bayan).
2
State the ruling for each
Consensus: habitual actions are permissible; exclusive actions are specific to him; explanatory actions carry the ruling of the explained text.
3
Analyze the tayammum example
The text discusses whether the verse on tayammum is ambiguous. Note the nuance: the author suggests that the verse might not be mujmal, but the action of wiping to the elbows clarifies the extent.
This is a point of discussion among scholars.
4
Understand the principle of bayan
An explanatory action takes the ruling of the text it explains. If the text is obligatory, following the action is obligatory; if recommended, then recommended.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل :
في بيان حكم أفعاله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم
مع
أن
الاتفاق في أفعاله (الجبلية الصادرة بمقتضى الطبيعة (الإباحة مطلقاً) في حقه صلوات الله وسلامه عليه وآله وأصحابه وفي حق الأمة (و) الاتفاق فيما خص به) بدليل (كالزيادة) على الأربع ( في النكاح والوصال في الصوم فإنه واصل ومنع أصحابه عنه، وقال: «إني لست كهيئتكم أبيت عند ربي يطعمني ويسقيني كما رويت في الصحاح» وصلاة التهجد عند من يقول بافتراضها عليه صلوات الله وسلامه عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه وغير ذلك (تخصيصه) به لا يشاركه فيها أمته (و) الاتفاق (فيما ظهر ) بياناً لمجمل (بقول) مثل: «صلوا كما رأيتموني أصلي») رواه البخاري، وفي كون هذا بياناً ما قد مر (أو قرينة كوقوعه بعد إجمال كالتيمم إلى المرافق) كما رواه الحاكم (الاعتبار بالمبين فإن خاصاً ،فخاص، وإن عاماً فعام، وإن وجوباً فوجوب، وإن ندباً أو إباحة فندب أو إباحة وهذا ظاهر جداً، لكن قد يناقش في مثال التيمم فإن آية التيمم هي قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِن كُنتُم مَّرْضَى أَوْ عَلَى سَفَرٍ أَوْ جَاءَ أَحَدٌ مِنكُم مِّنَ الْغَابِطِ أَوْ لَمَسْتُمُ النِّسَاءَ فَلَمْ تَجِدُوا مَاءً فَتَيَمَّمُوا صَعِيدًا طَيْبًا فَأَمَسَحُوا بِوُجُوهِكُمْ وَأَيْدِيكُم مِّنْهُ﴾ [المائدة: ٦] ليست مجملة حتى تحتاج إلى البيان كيف وقد مر من قبل أن مثل : فامسحوا برؤوسكم ليس ،مجملاً، وغاية ما يمكن أن يقال ليس المراد منه الإطلاق وإلا لزم أن يكفي مسح اصبع واحد أو شيء من الذراع، وهذا خلاف الإجماع، فالمراد منه قدر مخصوص من اليد الذي هو من الأصابع إلى الإبط، وهذا القدر مجهول وهو الإجمال، ثم وقوع هذا الفعل بياناً له لا يخلو عن كدر فإن الأحاديث فيها وقعت متعارضة كما لا يخفى على من تتبع كتب الحديث، وللتفصيل موضع آخر (وما سوى ذلك) من الأفعال فإن علم حكمه) من الوجوب والندب والإباحة (فالجمهور ومنهم الشيخ أبو بكر الجصاص التأسي واجب) فيتناول الحكم الأمة أيضاً (وقيل) التأسي واجب في العبادات خاصة دون غيرها (و) الشيخ أبو الحسن (الكرخي) منا ( والأشعرية) قالوا : (يخصه صلوات الله عليه وآله وأصحابه فلا يعم الأمة إلا بدليل) خاص معمم (وقول ابن الحاجب في تقرير المذاهب (وقيل) الفعل المعلوم الصفة (كالمجهول) الصفة (مجهول فإن فيه مذاهب كما سيأتي) ولا يتأتى جريان جميع تلك المذاهب ههنا، فلا بد من إرادة واحد وذا مجهول فتشبيه بالمجهول لنا أوّلاً الصحابة كانوا يرجعون إلى فعله احتجاجاً واقتداء وقد شاع وذاع في وقائع لا تحصى وهذا يفيد علماً عادياً بوجوب التأسي (قال) أمير المؤمنين (عمر ) رضي الله عنه في تقبيل الحجر : لولا أني رأيت النبي صلى
Chapter: On clarifying the ruling of his actions (peace and blessings be upon him and his family and companions). There is consensus on his habitual actions (which arise from nature) being absolutely permissible for him and the ummah. And consensus on what is specific to him by evidence (such as marrying more than four, continuous fasting). And consensus on what appears as an explanation of an ambiguous text, whether by statement (like 'Pray as you have seen me praying') or by context (like wiping to the elbows in tayammum). The ruling follows the explained thing: if specific, then specific; if general, then general; if obligatory, then obligatory; if recommended or permissible, then recommended or permissible. This is very apparent, but there is discussion about the example of tayammum. As for actions other than these, if their ruling is known (obligation, recommendation, permissibility), then the majority, including Abu Bakr al-Jassas, say following is obligatory, so the ruling applies to the ummah. It is said following is obligatory only in acts of worship. Al-Karkhi and the Ash'aris say it is specific to him, not general to the ummah without a specific generalizing proof. The statement of Ibn al-Hajib in presenting the schools: 'It is said the known action is like the unknown.' The opinion that the Companions used to refer to his action as evidence and follow it provides customary knowledge of the obligation to follow. Umar said about kissing the Black Stone: 'Had I not seen the Prophet...'
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage classifies the actions of the Prophet (peace be upon him) into several categories and discusses their legal implications for the ummah. First, habitual actions (jibaliyya) are universally considered permissible. Second, actions specific to the Prophet (like marrying more than four wives or continuous fasting) are exclusive to him. Third, actions that serve as explanations (bayan) of ambiguous texts carry the same ruling as the explained text. The author notes a subtlety in the example of tayammum, questioning whether the Quranic verse is truly ambiguous. Finally, for actions not falling into these categories, there is a debate: the majority (including Abu Bakr al-Jassas) hold that following the Prophet's actions is obligatory; others restrict this to acts of worship; and al-Karkhi with the Ash'aris hold that such actions are specific to him unless proven otherwise. The author sides with the majority, citing the practice of the Companions as evidence.

Key Terms
الجبلية (al-jibaliyya): Actions arising from human nature, such as standing, sitting, etc.
التأسي (al-ta'si): Following the Prophet's actions as a legal obligation
البيان (al-bayan): An action that explains an ambiguous (mujmal) text of the Quran or Sunnah

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling of the Prophet's habitual actions for the ummah?
A) Permissible
B) Obligatory
C) Haram
D) Recommended

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's actions are classified into habitual, exclusive, and explanatory, each with distinct rulings for the ummah.

The Obligation of Following the Prophet's Actions (al-Ta'si)
Dispute over the legal implication of the Prophet's non-categorized actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the dispute between the majority and al-Karkhi regarding the obligation of following the Prophet's actions, and evaluate the evidence for each position.

Core Idea
The majority holds that following the Prophet's non-explanatory, non-specific actions is obligatory; al-Karkhi says they are specific to him.

Beyond the clear categories, there is a debate: Are the Prophet's other actions binding on the ummah? The majority, including Abu Bakr al-Jassas, say yes, citing the Companions' practice. Others, like al-Karkhi, say no without a generalizing proof. This lesson dissects this dispute and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actions under dispute
These are the Prophet's actions that are not habitual, exclusive, or explanatory. Their ruling is not initially known from the categories.
2
State the majority position
The majority (including Abu Bakr al-Jassas) say following (al-ta'si) is obligatory for the ummah.
This is based on the Companions' practice.
3
State the opposing position
Al-Karkhi and the Ash'aris say such actions are specific to the Prophet and not general unless proven.
They require a specific dalil to extend the ruling.
4
Examine the evidence
The author presents the Companion's practice as providing 'customary knowledge' of obligation. Umar's statement about kissing the Black Stone is an example.
This is a dalil naqli (narrative evidence).
5
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: the majority would deem an action obligatory; al-Karkhi would not, until a generalizing proof is found.

Source Text

في بيان حكم أفعاله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم

مِّنْهُ﴾ [المائدة: ٦] ليست مجملة حتى تحتاج إلى البيان كيف وقد مر من قبل أن مثل : فامسحوا برؤوسكم ليس ،مجملاً، وغاية ما يمكن أن يقال ليس المراد منه الإطلاق وإلا لزم أن يكفي مسح اصبع واحد أو شيء من الذراع، وهذا خلاف الإجماع، فالمراد منه قدر مخصوص من اليد الذي هو من الأصابع إلى الإبط، وهذا القدر مجهول وهو الإجمال، ثم وقوع هذا الفعل بياناً له لا يخلو عن كدر فإن الأحاديث فيها وقعت متعارضة كما لا Chapter: On clarifying the ruling of his actions (peace and blessings be upon him and his family and companions). There is consensus on his habitual actions (which arise from nature) being absolutely permissible for him and the ummah. And consensus on what is specific to him by evidence (such as marrying more than four, continuous fasting). And consensus on what appears as an explanation of an ambiguous text, whether by statement (like 'Pray as you have seen me praying') or by context (like wiping to the elbows in tayammum). The ruling follows the explained thing: if specific, then specific; if general, then general; if obligatory, then obligatory; if recommended or permissible, then recommended or permissible. This is very apparent, but there is discussion about the example of tayammum. As for actions other than these, if their ruling is known (obligation, recommendation, permissibility), then the majority, including Abu Bakr al-Jassas, say following is obligatory, so the ruling applies to the ummah. It is said following is obligatory only in acts of worship. Al-Karkhi and the Ash'aris say it is specific to him, not general to the ummah without a specific generalizing proof. The statement of Ibn al-Hajib in presenting the schools: 'It is said the known action is like the unknown.' The opinion that the Companions used to refer to his action as evidence and follow it provides customary knowledge of the obligation to follow. Umar said about kissing the Black Stone: 'Had I not seen the Prophet...'
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage classifies the actions of the Prophet (peace be upon him) into several categories and discusses their legal implications for the ummah. First, habitual actions (jibaliyya) are universally considered permissible. Second, actions specific to the Prophet (like marrying more than four wives or continuous fasting) are exclusive to him. Third, actions that serve as explanations (bayan) of ambiguous texts carry the same ruling as the explained text. The author notes a subtlety in the example of tayammum, questioning whether the Quranic verse is truly ambiguous. Finally, for actions not falling into these categories, there is a debate: the majority (including Abu Bakr al-Jassas) hold that following the Prophet's actions is obligatory; others restrict this to acts of worship; and al-Karkhi with the Ash'aris hold that such actions are specific to him unless proven otherwise. The author sides with the majority, citing the practice of the Companions as evidence.

Key Terms
الجبلية (al-jibaliyya): Actions arising from human nature, such as standing, sitting, etc.
التأسي (al-ta'si): Following the Prophet's actions as a legal obligation
البيان (al-bayan): An action that explains an ambiguous (mujmal) text of the Quran or Sunnah

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why do the majority of scholars hold that following the Prophet's actions is obligatory?
A) Because the Quran commands it
B) Because the Companions consistently used his actions as evidence
C) Because al-Karkhi agreed
D) Because it is specific to him

Key Takeaway
The obligation to follow the Prophet's actions is established by the widespread practice of the Companions, not by a specific textual command.

Al-Sunnah as Source
intermediate
6 min
The Prophet's Actions as a Model: Ta'si and the Attribute Distinction
Dispute over the legal import of the Prophet's actions, focusing on the verse al-Ahzab 33:21.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi distinction between following the ruling of an act and following its attribute, and apply it to analyze the proof from al-Ahzab 33:21.

Core Idea
Ta'si obligates following the attribute, not necessarily the act's own ruling.

In this lesson, we examine one of the most debated usul al-fiqh questions: do the Prophet's actions (af'al al-nabi) impose obligation on the Ummah? We begin with the verse 'Indeed in the Messenger of Allah you have a good example' (al-Ahzab 33:21). Some scholars argue this makes every action of the Prophet binding. The Hanafi school responds by distinguishing between the act's own ruling and the attribute with which it is performed. Understanding this distinction is key to deriving correct rulings from the Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central proof
Locate the Quranic verse al-Ahzab 33:21 and the opponent's claim that it imposes obligation for every action of the Prophet.
The verse is 'Indeed in the Messenger of Allah you have a good example'.
2
Analyze the opponent's argument
Understand the reasoning: not following implies lack of faith, so following is obligatory. Also note the use of al-Hashiyah's rebuttal.
The opponent claims that the verse links faith to having a good example.
3
Examine the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: the obligation is to observe the attribute (sifah) of the act, not the act's own ruling. The act itself may be recommended or permissible.
This is analogous to acting upon a solitary report: the obligation is to follow the ruling conveyed, even if that ruling is recommendation or permissibility.
4
Apply to a scenario
Consider a supererogatory prayer follower behind an obligatory prayer imam. Does this violate 'following'? The Hanafi solution: following in form is sufficient for congregation; the pillars become obligatory due to the opening takbir and following.
The Shari'ah has specified that congregation requires following only in form (surah).
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: a Hanafi may perform an act whose ruling is unknown as permissible, while a Maliki considers it obligatory, and a Shafi'i considers it recommended.
This lesson focuses on the first part; the practical application will be expanded in lesson 2.

الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم يقبلك ما قبلتك) رواه الشيخان وصفة التقبيل كانت معلومة له رضي الله تعالى عنه، فإن مثله لا يغفل عن مثل هذا الحكم (و) لنا (ثانياً) قوله تعالى: ﴿لَقَدْ كانَ لَكُمْ فِي رَسُولِ اللَّهِ أَسْوَةٌ حَسَنَةٌ﴾ [الأحزاب: ٢١] فإن مفاده في العرف أن الأسوة لازمة متحققة على راجي الله واليوم الآخر، فيكون واجباً (والتأسي بالمثل) أي الإتيان بالمثل (صورة وصفة فيكون الفعل بوصفه عاماً للأمة أيضاً، وقد يقرر بأن مفاد الآية من كان مؤمناً بالله واليوم الآخر له أسوة حسنة، وهو يستلزم أن من ليس له أسوة حسنة ليس يؤمن بالله واليوم الآخر فيكون عدم الأسوة ملزوماً لعدم الإيمان، فيكون حراماً فتكون الصفة واجبة وهذا كما يقال : العمل على طبق خبر الواحد واجب، مع أن بعض الأخبار يفيد الندب والإباحة، يعني مراعاة حكم الخبر واجب، فكذا التأسي بمراعاة الصفة واجب فافهم (ومثله قوله تعالى: ﴿قُلْ إِن كُنتُمْ تُحِبُّونَ اللَّهَ فَاتَّبِعُونِي يُحْبِبْكُمُ اللهُ﴾ [آل عمران: ٣١] فإنه يفيد لزوم الإتباع، وهو الإتيان بالمثل صورة وصفة (أقول لو تم) هذا (لم يكن المتنفل المقتدي بالمفترض (متبعاً للمفترض (الإمام فلا عندنا بالتحريمة والاقتداء فوجد الاتباع صورة وصفة تأمل فيه واستدل بقوله تعالى: ﴿فَلَمَّا قَضَى زَيْدُ منها وطرا زَوَّجْنَكَهَا لِكَى لَا يَكُونَ عَلَى الْمُؤْمِنِينَ حَرَجٌ﴾ [الأحزاب: ٣٧] فإنه يدل على التشريك وجوداً وعدماً لأن مفاده أن الله تعالى أوقع التزوج ليستدل به على الإباحة، فلا يقعون في الحرج وهذا لا يكون بدون التشريك والتأسي قيل : إنما يتم هذا الدليل (لو علم جهة تزويجه من الوجوب أو غيره فإنه لو لم يعلم كان خارجاً عما نحن فيه (أقول : إباحة التزوج معلومة من التعليل بنفي الحرج ليكلا يلزم الاستدراك في العلة فإنه لو كان واجباً لكان الإتيان به ضرورياً لا مساغ للترك أصلاً، سواء أدى إلى الحرج أم لا فيكون التعليل مستدركاً (وفيه أنه يمكن أن يكون التزوج واجباً عليه إظهاراً لعدم الحرج على الأمة في زوج أدعيائهم، فيكون نفي الحرج علة الوجوب (وفيه أن في الإظهار بالقول لمندوحة فإنه يفيد الجواز (فلا فاقة إلى إيجاب الفعل) فلا يصلح نفي الحرج علة للإيجاب وفيه أن القول ينفي الحرج شرعاً لا طبعاً) فإن الإنسان كثيراً ما يتحرج عن فعل المباح لما رأى فيه من المداهنة أو تنفر الطبع (وفعل الرسول) المتبوع (ينفيهما معاً) فلا بعد في أن يوجب عليه الفعل نفياً لهذا الحرج (فتدبر) فإن الظاهر أن هذا كله مجادلة، والتزوج لم يكن واجباً عليه وإنما كان لميلان الطبع مباحاً كما يلوح من سياق القصة المروية في السير) (وإن جهل حكم الفعل من الوجوب والندب والإباحة (فباعتبار الأمة مذاهب الوجوب عليها وعليه مالك والندب وعليه الشافعي والإباحة وهو الصحيح عند أكثر الحنفية والمختار عند الشيخ أبي بكر الجصاص قدس سره وينبغي أن يكون ذلك عند عدم الدوام على مواظبة الفعل فإنه للوجوب عندهم كما يظهر من الهداية، فإنه استدل على وجوب صلاة العيدين بالمواظبة من غير ترك، لكن هذا غير مطرد فإن الجماعة سنة مؤكدة، أنه لم يتركهما أصلاً، وكذا الأذان والإقامة وصلاة الكسوف والخطبة الثانية في الجمعة والاعتكاف والترتيب والموالاة في الوضوء، وكذا المضمضة والاستنشاق وغير ذلك مما ثبت فيه المواظبة من غير ترك مع أنها سنة، وقد استدل هو نفسه على سنية أكثرها بالمواظبة مع عدم تبيين تركها، بل ثبت عدم الترك فتدبر أحسن التدبر فتعلم أن المواظبة ليست دليل الوجوب عندهم (و) ينبغي أيضاً أن يكون ذلك (عند عدم قرينة قصد القربة إذ لا قربة في مباح) وهو ظاهر (وهذا هو مختار (الآمدي من الشافعية والوقف وعليه الشيخ أبو الحسن (الكرخي) منا (والإمام الرازي) صاحب المحصول من الشافعية ونسب إلى أكثر الأشعرية) أصحاب الوجوب (قالوا : أولاً) قال تعالى: ﴿وَمَا آتَاكُمُ الرَّسُولُ فَخُذُوهُ﴾ [الحشر: ٧] والأمر للوجوب إلا بدليل صارف فالأخذ بالفعل واجب (والجواب المعنى ما أمركم) به فخذوه (لمقابلة وما نهاكم عنه فانتهوا (و) قالوا (ثانياً) قال تعالى (فاتبعوا) والاتباع الإتيان بالمثل (فيجب المثل الجواب المراد الاتباع في العقائد العلمية والعملية (أو الإتباع في الواجبات المعلومة) والآية ليست مبقاة على العموم فإن الأفعال المباحة لا يجب الإتيان بها (كيف لا و) إلا (يلزم على كل تقدير من وجوب فعل مثل كل ما فعل الضدان بالنسبة إلينا الوجوب والندب أو الإباحة (إذا فعله في نفس الأمر على وجه الإباحة أو الندب لأن التأسي في كما الصفة ضروري فيكون مباحاً أو مندوباً وقد أوجبتم في هذه الصورة الوجوب فوجب الضدان (وأورد) عليه (منع كونه مباحاً على تقدير أن لا يعلم (جهته أي إن كان الفعل المفعول بالإباحة غير معلوم الجهة يمنع كونه مباحاً علينا، بل هو واجب (و) منع (كونه واجباً على تقدير أن يعلم) يعني إن كان معلوم الجهة لا يكون واجباً أقول الإباحة والوجوب مفروضان أما الإباحة فلأنه فرض الفعل مباحاً عليه صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم والإباحة عليه توجب الإباحة علينا في نفس الأمر، وأما الوجوب فلما قلتم إن الفعل الغير المعلوم الجهة واجب علينا (ومنع المفروض لا يجوز) وفيه أن مقصود المورد أن الإباحة الغير المعلومة لا توجب الإباحة، وإذا كانت معلومة فلا وجوب، فلا يلزم الضدان، فليس الإباحة في غير المعلوم مفروضاً ولا الوجوب في المعلوم مفروضاً، وفي الحاشية أن التزام هذا بعيد عن الإنصاف، ثم إن مثل هذا يرد على قائلي الإباحة بأن الفعل إن كان على جهة الوجوب يلزم اجتماع الضدين فيجاب عنه بأن الفعل على جهة الوجوب مع عدم العلم به مما لا يجوز فإن العادة الشريفة زيادة الاهتمام بحال الواجب وتبيينه وتأكيده فيه نعم يرد أن الوجوب بالغير وهو الاتباع لا ينافي الإباحة لذاته) يعني أنا نلتزم اجتماع الإباحة والوجوب لكن الإباحة بالنظر إلى نفس الفعل والوجوب لأجل الاتباع، فلا محذور كالأكل المحلوف عليه فإنه بنفسه مباح ولكونه إبفاء لليمين واجب فتأمل (و) قالوا (ثالثاً) إن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم خلع نعليه في الصلاة، فجعلوا يخلعون نعالهم فقال: «ما حملكم على أن ألقيتم نعالكم قالوا : خلعت فخلعنا) والمحفوظ رأيناك
That the second principle / the Sunnah. ... He (the Prophet) kissed what you kissed? Both Bukhari and Muslim narrated it, and the manner of kissing was known to him (the Companion), may Allah be pleased with him, for someone like him does not neglect such a ruling. (And) for us (secondly) His statement, the Most High: 'Indeed in the Messenger of Allah you have a good example' [Al-Ahzab:21] for its import in custom is that the example is binding and realized for one who hopes for Allah and the Last Day, so it is obligatory. (And following in the like) i.e., doing the like (in form and attribute, so the act with its attribute is general for the Ummah as well. And it may be established that the import of the verse is that whoever believes in Allah and the Last Day has a good example, which entails that whoever does not have a good example does not believe in Allah and the Last Day, so lack of following becomes concomitant with lack of faith, thus it is prohibited, so following is obligatory. He said in al-Hashiyah: There is something in it, perhaps its point is that it entails the obligation of following, even if the attribute were of recommendation or permissibility, and this is nothing. For following (ta'si) i.e., performing according to the attribute of recommendation or permissibility is obligatory, meaning observing the attribute is obligatory. This is like saying: acting according to a solitary report is obligatory, even though some reports indicate recommendation and permissibility, meaning observing the ruling of the report is obligatory. Similarly, following by observing the attribute is obligatory, so understand. (And similar to it is His statement, the Most High: 'Say, if you love Allah, then follow me, Allah will love you' [Aal-i-Imran:31] for it indicates the necessity of following, which is doing the like in form and attribute. (I say if this were complete) then the supererogatory prayer performer following the obligatory prayer performer would not be following the obligatory (imam), so this following would not be permissible, because following is its condition. (And it is not far-fetched to rebut by the immediate understanding, and it is said that the immediate understanding from following (ta'si) and following (ittiba) is doing the like in form and attribute, but the congregation is specified by following in form by sacred law. Also, the pillars of prayer become obligatory according to us by the opening takbir and following, so following in form and attribute is found. Ponder. And he argued with His statement, the Most High: 'So when Zayd had completed his need from her, We married her to you so that there may be no difficulty for the believers' [Al-Ahzab:37] for it indicates sharing in existence and non-existence, because its import is that Allah, the Most High, caused the marriage to occur so that it may be used as evidence for permissibility, so that they do not fall into difficulty. And this cannot be without sharing and following. It was said: This proof is only complete if the aspect of his marriage from obligation or other is known, for if it is not known, it is outside what we are discussing. (I say: The permissibility of marriage is known from the rationale of negating difficulty, otherwise there would be redundancy in the cause, for if it were obligatory, performing it would be necessary with no room for omission at all, whether it leads to difficulty or not, so the rationale would be redundant. (And there is that it is possible that the marriage was obligatory upon him to manifest the lack of difficulty for the Ummah regarding marrying the wives of their adopted sons, so the negation of difficulty is the cause of obligation. (And there is that in manifesting by speech there is ample scope, for it indicates permissibility, so there is no need to make the act obligatory, so the negation of difficulty is not suitable as a cause for obligation. And there is that speech negates difficulty legally, not naturally; for a person often feels difficulty about a permissible act because he sees in it dissimulation or natural aversion. And the act of the Prophet, the one followed, negates both, so it is not far-fetched that the act be obligatory upon him to negate this difficulty. (So ponder), for apparently all this is disputation, and marriage was not obligatory upon him, but was permissible due to natural inclination, as appears from the context of the story narrated in the biographies. (And if the ruling of the act from obligation, recommendation, or permissibility is unknown, then with respect to the Ummah there are schools: obligation upon them and upon it Malik, recommendation upon them and upon it al-Shafi'i, and permissibility which is the correct view according to most Hanafis and the chosen view of Sheikh Abu Bakr al-Jassas, may Allah sanctify his soul. And this should be when there is no continuity of persevering upon the act, for it indicates obligation according to them as appears from al-Hidayah, for he argued for the obligation of the two Eid prayers by perseverance without leaving, but this is not uniform, for congregational prayer is a confirmed sunnah, yet he never left it at all. Likewise, the adhan, iqamah, eclipse prayer, the second sermon on Friday, i'tikaf, order, and continuity in wudu, likewise rinsing the mouth and nostrils, and other things in which perseverance without leaving is established, yet they are sunnah. He himself argued for the sunnah nature of most of them by perseverance without specifying leaving them; rather, non-leaving is established. So ponder most beautifully, then you will know that perseverance is not evidence of obligation according to them. (And) it should also be that (when there is no indication of intention of nearness, for there is no nearness in permissible), which is apparent. (And this is the chosen view of al-Amidi among the Shafi'is and the pause, and upon it is Sheikh Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi from us, and Imam al-Razi, author of al-Mahsul among the Shafi'is, and it is attributed to most Ash'aris) the proponents of obligation (They said: First) He, the Most High, said: 'And whatever the Messenger gives you, take it' [al-Hashr:7] and the command is for obligation unless a diverting proof, so taking the act is obligatory. (The answer: The meaning is 'whatever He commands you with, take it' in contrast to 'And whatever He forbids you from, refrain'. (And) they said (secondly) He, the Most High, said: 'Follow (fa-ttabi'u)' and following is doing the like, so the like is obligatory. (The answer: The intended following is in intellectual and practical beliefs, (or following in known obligations) and the verse is not left on its generality, for permissible acts are not obligatory to perform. (How not? otherwise) it would entail in every estimation the obligation of doing the like of every act, the two opposites relative to us: obligation and recommendation or permissibility (if he did it in reality on the basis of permissibility or recommendation, because following in the attribute is necessary, so it would be permissible or recommended, and you have made obligation in this case, so the two opposites would be necessary. (And it was objected) to him (by preventing it from being permissible on the estimation that its aspect is not known, i.e., if the act done with permissibility is unknown in aspect, it is prevented from being permissible for us; rather, it is obligatory. (And) preventing (it from being obligatory on the estimation that it is known), i.e., if its aspect is known, it is not obligatory. I say: Permissibility and obligation are assumed. As for permissibility, because he assumed the act permissible upon him, peace be upon him, and permissibility upon him entails permissibility upon us in reality. As for obligation, because you said that the act whose aspect is unknown is obligatory upon us. (And preventing the assumed is not permissible.) There is that the objector's intent is that permissibility which is unknown does not entail permissibility, and if it is known, there is no obligation, so the two opposites are not necessary. So permissibility in the unknown is not assumed, nor is obligation in the known assumed. In al-Hashiyah: Committing to this is far from fairness. Then, similar to this is objected to the proponents of permissibility by that if the act is on the basis of obligation, the conjunction of two opposites would be necessary. It is answered that the act on the basis of obligation without knowledge of it is not permissible, for the noble custom is increased attention to the state of the obligatory and its clarification and emphasis. Yes, it is objected that obligation due to another, i.e., following, does not contradict permissibility in itself.) Meaning we commit to the conjunction of permissibility and obligation, but permissibility with respect to the act itself and obligation due to following, so there is no problem, like eating something sworn upon: it is in itself permissible, but because it fulfills the oath, it is obligatory. So ponder. (And) they said (thirdly) that the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, removed his sandals in prayer, so they began to remove their sandals. He said: 'What made you throw your sandals?' They said: 'You removed, so we removed.' And the preserved version is: 'We saw you...'
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul al-fiqh dispute concerning the legal import of the Prophet's actions (af'al al-nabi) and whether they impose obligation (wujub), recommendation (nadb), or mere permissibility (ibahah) upon the Ummah. The author presents multiple proofs and counter-proofs, reflecting the Hanafi school's nuanced position.

The discussion begins with the verse 'Indeed in the Messenger of Allah you have a good example' (al-Ahzab:21). Some argue that this verse makes it obligatory to follow the Prophet in every action, including the specific manner (sifah) of the act. The author explains that the concept of 'uswa' (example) implies obligation, as it is linked to faith in Allah and the Last Day. However, a rebuttal from al-Hashiyah points out that even if the act itself is recommended or permissible, the obligation to follow lies in observing the attribute (sifah) of the act, not in the act's own ruling. This is analogous to acting upon a solitary report (khabar al-wahid): the obligation is to follow the ruling it conveys, even if that ruling is recommendation or permissibility.

The discussion then moves to the verse 'Say, if you love Allah, then follow me' (Aal-i-Imran:31). The author raises a problem: if following the Prophet in every attribute is obligatory, then a person praying a supererogatory prayer behind an imam praying an obligatory prayer would not be truly 'following' him, since the imam's prayer is obligatory while the follower's is supererogatory. This could invalidate the congregation. The author suggests a resolution: the immediate understanding (tabadul) from 'following' is doing the like in form and attribute, but the Shari'ah has specified that congregation requires following only in form (surah), not in the ruling of the act. Moreover, in the Hanafi school, the actions of the prayer become obligatory for the follower due to the opening takbir and the act of following, so the requirement of following in both form and attribute is actually fulfilled.

Next, the author examines the verse about the Prophet's marriage to Zaynab bint Jahsh (al-Ahzab:37). The argument is that Allah caused this marriage to occur to remove any difficulty (haraj) for the believers in marrying the wives of their adopted sons. This indicates that the Prophet's action serves as a model (tashrik) for permissibility. However, critics say this only works if the legal aspect (jihah) of the marriage—whether it was obligatory, recommended, or permissible for the Prophet—is known. If unknown, it cannot be used as proof. The author responds that the permissibility of the marriage is clear from the rationale (illah) of negating difficulty, but then engages in a dialectical back-and-forth, ultimately concluding that the marriage was not obligatory for the Prophet but permissible, as indicated by the biography.

The passage then turns to the situation where the ruling of an act is unknown: whether it was obligatory, recommended, or permissible for the Prophet. Three positions are presented: (1) It is obligatory for the Ummah (attributed to Imam Malik and some Hanafis), (2) It is recommended (attributed to al-Shafi'i), and (3) It is permissible (the correct view according to most Hanafis and chosen by Abu Bakr al-Jassas). The author clarifies that the criterion is whether the Prophet consistently persevered (muwazabah) upon the act without ever leaving it. If so, some argue it indicates obligation. But the author critiques this by giving counterexamples: congregational prayer, adhan, iqamah, etc., are all performed consistently yet are sunnah, not obligatory. Thus, mere perseverance is not proof of obligation; a clear sign (qarinah) of intending nearness (qurbah) is needed, and permissible acts lack this.

The proponents of obligation then bring two Quranic proofs: (1) 'Whatever the Messenger gives you, take it' (al-Hashr:7), arguing that the command to 'take' implies obligation; the answer is that it refers to his commands, not his actions. (2) The command 'Follow' (fa-ttabi'u) in Aal-i-Imran:31. The answer is that the command pertains to obligations already known, not to every action, otherwise it would force the conjunction of opposites (obligation and recommendation/permissibility) if the Prophet's act is recommended/permissible. The objectors claim that if the act's aspect is unknown, it must be obligatory for us; if known, it cannot be obligatory. The author rejoins by distinguishing between the act's own ruling (which may be permissible) and the obligation to follow due to the command of following—an obligation arising from an external factor (wujub bil-ghayr). This does not contradict the act's inherent permissibility, just as eating something sworn upon is inherently permissible but becomes obligatory to fulfill the oath.

Finally, a hadith is cited: the Prophet removed his sandals in prayer, and the Companions did the same. When asked why, they said 'You removed, so we removed.' This shows immediate following. The passage ends with 'al-mahfuz ra'aynaka', indicating the narration continues.

The overall Hanafi position is that the Prophet's actions do not automatically impose obligation; they can indicate recommendation or permissibility depending on context, perseverance, and the presence of an intention of nearness. The relied-upon view among the Hanafis is that when the ruling is unknown, the act is permissible for the Ummah, unless there is evidence of obligation. This lesson illustrates the sophisticated usul methodology of the Hanafi school in deriving rulings from the Sunnah.

Key Terms
التأسي (al-ta'si): Following the Prophet by doing the like of his action in form and attribute (surah wa sifah).
الأسوة (al-uswah): The example set by the Prophet that serves as a model for the Ummah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in this lesson, what does the verse 'Indeed in the Messenger of Allah you have a good example' obligate?
A) It obligates the Ummah to perform every action of the Prophet exactly as he did.
B) It obligates the Ummah to observe the attribute (sifah) of the Prophet's actions, but the act itself may be recommended or permissible.
C) It obligates the Ummah to only follow the Prophet's commands, not his actions.
D) It does not obligate anything; it is merely a statement of fact.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between the ruling of an act and its attribute; following the attribute is obligatory even when the act itself is recommended or permissible.

Al-Sunnah as Source
intermediate
8 min
The Ruling of the Prophet's Acts When Unknown: Hanafi Permissibility vs. Obligation and Recommendation
Dispute over the ruling of the Prophet's actions when unknown, with analysis of muwazabah and additional proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi view that when the ruling of a Prophet's action is unknown, it is permissible for the Ummah, and distinguish it from the Maliki and Shafi'i positions.

Core Idea
When the hukm of a Prophet's act is unknown, the Hanafi default is permissibility, not obligation.

In this continuation, we explore the scenario where the legal ruling (hukm) of a Prophet's action is not known—whether it was wajib, mandub, or mubah for him. Three schools offer different answers: Malik says obligation, Shafi'i says recommendation, and the Hanafi majority say permissibility. The text also examines key counterarguments regarding perseverance (muwazabah) and the nature of following. By the end, you will see how the Hanafi school relies on contextual evidence and the absence of qarinah to reach its conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
Distinguish the views of Malik (obligation), al-Shafi'i (recommendation), and the Hanafi majority (permissibility) when the ruling of the Prophet's action is unknown.
The text states that the correct view is permissibility according to most Hanafis and the chosen view of Abu Bakr al-Jassas.
2
Examine the argument from muwazabah
Some argue consistent performance without leaving indicates obligation. The text rebuts with counterexamples: congregational prayer, adhan, iqamah, etc., are consistently performed yet are sunnah.
Muwazabah alone is not evidence of wujub; a qarinah of intended nearness is needed.
3
Analyze the additional Quranic proofs
The opponents cite al-Hashr 7 ('take what the Messenger gives') and Aal-i-Imran 31 ('Follow me'). The Hanafi responses: al-Hashr 7 refers to commands, and 'Follow' is restricted to known obligations.
These verses are interpreted narrowly to avoid generalizing obligation to all actions.
4
Resolve the conjunction of opposites argument
If the act is permissible for the Prophet but we are obligated to follow, does that create two opposite rulings? The Hanafi answer: obligation arises from the command to follow (wujub bil-ghayr), not from the act itself.
This is similar to eating something sworn upon: permissible in itself but obligatory to fulfill the oath.
5
Apply the hadith of the sandals
The Companions immediately removed their sandals upon seeing the Prophet do so in prayer, showing that following is natural, but the ruling must be derived from evidence.
The hadith illustrates the immediate understanding (tabadul) but does not settle the legal ruling.
