أن الأصل الثاني/ السنة
الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم يقبلك ما قبلتك) رواه الشيخان وصفة التقبيل كانت معلومة له رضي الله تعالى عنه، فإن مثله لا يغفل عن مثل هذا الحكم (و) لنا (ثانياً) قوله تعالى: ﴿لَقَدْ كانَ لَكُمْ فِي رَسُولِ اللَّهِ أَسْوَةٌ حَسَنَةٌ﴾ [الأحزاب: ٢١] فإن مفاده في العرف أن الأسوة لازمة متحققة على راجي الله واليوم الآخر، فيكون واجباً (والتأسي بالمثل) أي الإتيان بالمثل (صورة وصفة فيكون الفعل بوصفه عاماً للأمة أيضاً، وقد يقرر بأن مفاد الآية من كان مؤمناً بالله واليوم الآخر له أسوة حسنة، وهو يستلزم أن من ليس له أسوة حسنة ليس يؤمن بالله واليوم الآخر فيكون عدم الأسوة ملزوماً لعدم الإيمان، فيكون حراماً فتكون الأسوة واجبة ، قال في «الحاشية»: وفيه ما فيه، ولعل وجهه أنه يلزم منه وجوب التأسي، ولو كان الصفة صفة الندب أو الإباحة، وهذا ليس بشيء، فإن التأسي أي الإتيان على صفة الندب أو الإباحة واجب يعني مراعاة الصفة واجبة وهذا كما يقال : العمل على طبق خبر الواحد واجب، مع أن بعض الأخبار يفيد الندب والإباحة، يعني مراعاة حكم الخبر واجب، فكذا التأسي بمراعاة الصفة واجب فافهم (ومثله قوله تعالى: ﴿قُلْ إِن كُنتُمْ تُحِبُّونَ اللَّهَ فَاتَّبِعُونِي يُحْبِبْكُمُ اللهُ﴾ [آل عمران: ٣١] فإنه يفيد لزوم الإتباع، وهو الإتيان بالمثل صورة وصفة (أقول لو تم) هذا (لم يكن المتنفل المقتدي بالمفترض (متبعاً للمفترض (الإمام فلا يجوز هذا الاقتداء، لأن الاتباع شرطه (ولا يبعد أن يدفع بالتبادر ويقال : بأن التبادر من التأسي والاتباع الإتيان بالمثل صورة وصفة، ولكن خص الجماعة بالاتباع صورة شرعاً، وأيضاً الأركان الصلاتية تصير واجبة عندنا بالتحريمة والاقتداء فوجد الاتباع صورة وصفة تأمل فيه واستدل بقوله تعالى: ﴿فَلَمَّا قَضَى زَيْدُ منها وطرا زَوَّجْنَكَهَا لِكَى لَا يَكُونَ عَلَى الْمُؤْمِنِينَ حَرَجٌ﴾ [الأحزاب: ٣٧] فإنه يدل على التشريك وجوداً وعدماً لأن مفاده أن الله تعالى أوقع التزوج ليستدل به على الإباحة، فلا يقعون في الحرج وهذا لا يكون بدون التشريك والتأسي قيل : إنما يتم هذا الدليل (لو علم جهة تزويجه من الوجوب أو غيره فإنه لو لم يعلم كان خارجاً عما نحن فيه (أقول : إباحة التزوج معلومة من التعليل بنفي الحرج ليكلا يلزم الاستدراك في العلة فإنه لو كان واجباً لكان الإتيان به ضرورياً لا مساغ للترك أصلاً، سواء أدى إلى الحرج أم لا فيكون التعليل مستدركاً (وفيه أنه يمكن أن يكون التزوج واجباً عليه إظهاراً لعدم الحرج على الأمة في زوج أدعيائهم، فيكون نفي الحرج علة الوجوب (وفيه أن في الإظهار بالقول لمندوحة فإنه يفيد الجواز (فلا فاقة إلى إيجاب الفعل) فلا يصلح نفي الحرج علة للإيجاب وفيه أن القول ينفي الحرج شرعاً لا طبعاً) فإن الإنسان كثيراً ما يتحرج عن فعل المباح لما رأى فيه من المداهنة أو تنفر الطبع (وفعل الرسول) المتبوع (ينفيهما معاً) فلا بعد في أن يوجب عليه الفعل نفياً لهذا الحرج (فتدبر) فإن الظاهر أن هذا كله مجادلة، والتزوج لم يكن واجباً عليه وإنما كان لميلان الطبع مباحاً كما يلوح من سياق القصة المروية في السير) (وإن جهل حكم الفعل من الوجوب والندب والإباحة (فباعتبار الأمة مذاهب الوجوب عليها وعليه مالك والندب وعليه الشافعي والإباحة وهو الصحيح عند أكثر الحنفية والمختار عند الشيخ أبي بكر الجصاص قدس سره وينبغي أن يكون ذلك عند عدم الدوام على مواظبة الفعل فإنه للوجوب عندهم كما يظهر من الهداية، فإنه استدل على وجوب صلاة العيدين بالمواظبة من غير ترك، لكن هذا غير مطرد فإن الجماعة سنة مؤكدة، أنه لم يتركهما أصلاً، وكذا الأذان والإقامة وصلاة الكسوف والخطبة الثانية في الجمعة والاعتكاف والترتيب والموالاة في الوضوء، وكذا المضمضة والاستنشاق وغير ذلك مما ثبت فيه المواظبة من غير ترك مع أنها سنة، وقد استدل هو نفسه على سنية أكثرها بالمواظبة مع عدم تبيين تركها، بل ثبت عدم الترك فتدبر أحسن التدبر فتعلم أن المواظبة ليست دليل الوجوب عندهم (و) ينبغي أيضاً أن يكون ذلك (عند عدم قرينة قصد القربة إذ لا قربة في مباح) وهو ظاهر (وهذا هو مختار (الآمدي من الشافعية والوقف وعليه الشيخ أبو الحسن (الكرخي) منا (والإمام الرازي) صاحب المحصول من الشافعية ونسب إلى أكثر الأشعرية) أصحاب الوجوب (قالوا : أولاً) قال تعالى: ﴿وَمَا آتَاكُمُ الرَّسُولُ فَخُذُوهُ﴾ [الحشر: ٧] والأمر للوجوب إلا بدليل صارف فالأخذ بالفعل واجب (والجواب المعنى ما أمركم) به فخذوه (لمقابلة وما نهاكم عنه فانتهوا (و) قالوا (ثانياً) قال تعالى (فاتبعوا) والاتباع الإتيان بالمثل (فيجب المثل الجواب المراد الاتباع في العقائد العلمية والعملية (أو الإتباع في الواجبات المعلومة) والآية ليست مبقاة على العموم فإن الأفعال المباحة لا يجب الإتيان بها (كيف لا و) إلا (يلزم على كل تقدير من وجوب فعل مثل كل ما فعل الضدان بالنسبة إلينا الوجوب والندب أو الإباحة (إذا فعله في نفس الأمر على وجه الإباحة أو الندب لأن التأسي في كما الصفة ضروري فيكون مباحاً أو مندوباً وقد أوجبتم في هذه الصورة الوجوب فوجب الضدان (وأورد) عليه (منع كونه مباحاً على تقدير أن لا يعلم (جهته أي إن كان الفعل المفعول بالإباحة غير معلوم الجهة يمنع كونه مباحاً علينا، بل هو واجب (و) منع (كونه واجباً على تقدير أن يعلم) يعني إن كان معلوم الجهة لا يكون واجباً أقول الإباحة والوجوب مفروضان أما الإباحة فلأنه فرض الفعل مباحاً عليه صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم والإباحة عليه توجب الإباحة علينا في نفس الأمر، وأما الوجوب فلما قلتم إن الفعل الغير المعلوم الجهة واجب علينا (ومنع المفروض لا يجوز) وفيه أن مقصود المورد أن الإباحة الغير المعلومة لا توجب الإباحة، وإذا كانت معلومة فلا وجوب، فلا يلزم الضدان، فليس الإباحة في غير المعلوم مفروضاً ولا الوجوب في المعلوم مفروضاً، وفي الحاشية أن التزام هذا بعيد عن الإنصاف، ثم إن مثل هذا يرد على قائلي الإباحة بأن الفعل إن كان على جهة الوجوب يلزم اجتماع الضدين فيجاب عنه بأن الفعل على جهة الوجوب مع عدم العلم به مما لا يجوز فإن العادة الشريفة زيادة الاهتمام بحال الواجب وتبيينه وتأكيده فيه نعم يرد أن الوجوب بالغير وهو الاتباع لا ينافي الإباحة لذاته) يعني أنا نلتزم اجتماع الإباحة والوجوب لكن الإباحة بالنظر إلى نفس الفعل والوجوب لأجل الاتباع، فلا محذور كالأكل المحلوف عليه فإنه بنفسه مباح ولكونه إبفاء لليمين واجب فتأمل (و) قالوا (ثالثاً) إن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم خلع نعليه في الصلاة، فجعلوا يخلعون نعالهم فقال: «ما حملكم على أن ألقيتم نعالكم قالوا : خلعت فخلعنا) والمحفوظ رأيناك
That the second principle / the Sunnah. ... He (the Prophet) kissed what you kissed? Both Bukhari and Muslim narrated it, and the manner of kissing was known to him (the Companion), may Allah be pleased with him, for someone like him does not neglect such a ruling. (And) for us (secondly) His statement, the Most High: 'Indeed in the Messenger of Allah you have a good example' [Al-Ahzab:21] for its import in custom is that the example is binding and realized for one who hopes for Allah and the Last Day, so it is obligatory. (And following in the like) i.e., doing the like (in form and attribute, so the act with its attribute is general for the Ummah as well. And it may be established that the import of the verse is that whoever believes in Allah and the Last Day has a good example, which entails that whoever does not have a good example does not believe in Allah and the Last Day, so lack of following becomes concomitant with lack of faith, thus it is prohibited, so following is obligatory. He said in al-Hashiyah: There is something in it, perhaps its point is that it entails the obligation of following, even if the attribute were of recommendation or permissibility, and this is nothing. For following (ta'si) i.e., performing according to the attribute of recommendation or permissibility is obligatory, meaning observing the attribute is obligatory. This is like saying: acting according to a solitary report is obligatory, even though some reports indicate recommendation and permissibility, meaning observing the ruling of the report is obligatory. Similarly, following by observing the attribute is obligatory, so understand. (And similar to it is His statement, the Most High: 'Say, if you love Allah, then follow me, Allah will love you' [Aal-i-Imran:31] for it indicates the necessity of following, which is doing the like in form and attribute. (I say if this were complete) then the supererogatory prayer performer following the obligatory prayer performer would not be following the obligatory (imam), so this following would not be permissible, because following is its condition. (And it is not far-fetched to rebut by the immediate understanding, and it is said that the immediate understanding from following (ta'si) and following (ittiba) is doing the like in form and attribute, but the congregation is specified by following in form by sacred law. Also, the pillars of prayer become obligatory according to us by the opening takbir and following, so following in form and attribute is found. Ponder. And he argued with His statement, the Most High: 'So when Zayd had completed his need from her, We married her to you so that there may be no difficulty for the believers' [Al-Ahzab:37] for it indicates sharing in existence and non-existence, because its import is that Allah, the Most High, caused the marriage to occur so that it may be used as evidence for permissibility, so that they do not fall into difficulty. And this cannot be without sharing and following. It was said: This proof is only complete if the aspect of his marriage from obligation or other is known, for if it is not known, it is outside what we are discussing. (I say: The permissibility of marriage is known from the rationale of negating difficulty, otherwise there would be redundancy in the cause, for if it were obligatory, performing it would be necessary with no room for omission at all, whether it leads to difficulty or not, so the rationale would be redundant. (And there is that it is possible that the marriage was obligatory upon him to manifest the lack of difficulty for the Ummah regarding marrying the wives of their adopted sons, so the negation of difficulty is the cause of obligation. (And there is that in manifesting by speech there is ample scope, for it indicates permissibility, so there is no need to make the act obligatory, so the negation of difficulty is not suitable as a cause for obligation. And there is that speech negates difficulty legally, not naturally; for a person often feels difficulty about a permissible act because he sees in it dissimulation or natural aversion. And the act of the Prophet, the one followed, negates both, so it is not far-fetched that the act be obligatory upon him to negate this difficulty. (So ponder), for apparently all this is disputation, and marriage was not obligatory upon him, but was permissible due to natural inclination, as appears from the context of the story narrated in the biographies. (And if the ruling of the act from obligation, recommendation, or permissibility is unknown, then with respect to the Ummah there are schools: obligation upon them and upon it Malik, recommendation upon them and upon it al-Shafi'i, and permissibility which is the correct view according to most Hanafis and the chosen view of Sheikh Abu Bakr al-Jassas, may Allah sanctify his soul. And this should be when there is no continuity of persevering upon the act, for it indicates obligation according to them as appears from al-Hidayah, for he argued for the obligation of the two Eid prayers by perseverance without leaving, but this is not uniform, for congregational prayer is a confirmed sunnah, yet he never left it at all. Likewise, the adhan, iqamah, eclipse prayer, the second sermon on Friday, i'tikaf, order, and continuity in wudu, likewise rinsing the mouth and nostrils, and other things in which perseverance without leaving is established, yet they are sunnah. He himself argued for the sunnah nature of most of them by perseverance without specifying leaving them; rather, non-leaving is established. So ponder most beautifully, then you will know that perseverance is not evidence of obligation according to them. (And) it should also be that (when there is no indication of intention of nearness, for there is no nearness in permissible), which is apparent. (And this is the chosen view of al-Amidi among the Shafi'is and the pause, and upon it is Sheikh Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi from us, and Imam al-Razi, author of al-Mahsul among the Shafi'is, and it is attributed to most Ash'aris) the proponents of obligation (They said: First) He, the Most High, said: 'And whatever the Messenger gives you, take it' [al-Hashr:7] and the command is for obligation unless a diverting proof, so taking the act is obligatory. (The answer: The meaning is 'whatever He commands you with, take it' in contrast to 'And whatever He forbids you from, refrain'. (And) they said (secondly) He, the Most High, said: 'Follow (fa-ttabi'u)' and following is doing the like, so the like is obligatory. (The answer: The intended following is in intellectual and practical beliefs, (or following in known obligations) and the verse is not left on its generality, for permissible acts are not obligatory to perform. (How not? otherwise) it would entail in every estimation the obligation of doing the like of every act, the two opposites relative to us: obligation and recommendation or permissibility (if he did it in reality on the basis of permissibility or recommendation, because following in the attribute is necessary, so it would be permissible or recommended, and you have made obligation in this case, so the two opposites would be necessary. (And it was objected) to him (by preventing it from being permissible on the estimation that its aspect is not known, i.e., if the act done with permissibility is unknown in aspect, it is prevented from being permissible for us; rather, it is obligatory. (And) preventing (it from being obligatory on the estimation that it is known), i.e., if its aspect is known, it is not obligatory. I say: Permissibility and obligation are assumed. As for permissibility, because he assumed the act permissible upon him, peace be upon him, and permissibility upon him entails permissibility upon us in reality. As for obligation, because you said that the act whose aspect is unknown is obligatory upon us. (And preventing the assumed is not permissible.) There is that the objector's intent is that permissibility which is unknown does not entail permissibility, and if it is known, there is no obligation, so the two opposites are not necessary. So permissibility in the unknown is not assumed, nor is obligation in the known assumed. In al-Hashiyah: Committing to this is far from fairness. Then, similar to this is objected to the proponents of permissibility by that if the act is on the basis of obligation, the conjunction of two opposites would be necessary. It is answered that the act on the basis of obligation without knowledge of it is not permissible, for the noble custom is increased attention to the state of the obligatory and its clarification and emphasis. Yes, it is objected that obligation due to another, i.e., following, does not contradict permissibility in itself.) Meaning we commit to the conjunction of permissibility and obligation, but permissibility with respect to the act itself and obligation due to following, so there is no problem, like eating something sworn upon: it is in itself permissible, but because it fulfills the oath, it is obligatory. So ponder. (And) they said (thirdly) that the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, removed his sandals in prayer, so they began to remove their sandals. He said: 'What made you throw your sandals?' They said: 'You removed, so we removed.' And the preserved version is: 'We saw you...'
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul al-fiqh dispute concerning the legal import of the Prophet's actions (af'al al-nabi) and whether they impose obligation (wujub), recommendation (nadb), or mere permissibility (ibahah) upon the Ummah. The author presents multiple proofs and counter-proofs, reflecting the Hanafi school's nuanced position.

The discussion begins with the verse 'Indeed in the Messenger of Allah you have a good example' (al-Ahzab:21). Some argue that this verse makes it obligatory to follow the Prophet in every action, including the specific manner (sifah) of the act. The author explains that the concept of 'uswa' (example) implies obligation, as it is linked to faith in Allah and the Last Day. However, a rebuttal from al-Hashiyah points out that even if the act itself is recommended or permissible, the obligation to follow lies in observing the attribute (sifah) of the act, not in the act's own ruling. This is analogous to acting upon a solitary report (khabar al-wahid): the obligation is to follow the ruling it conveys, even if that ruling is recommendation or permissibility.

The discussion then moves to the verse 'Say, if you love Allah, then follow me' (Aal-i-Imran:31). The author raises a problem: if following the Prophet in every attribute is obligatory, then a person praying a supererogatory prayer behind an imam praying an obligatory prayer would not be truly 'following' him, since the imam's prayer is obligatory while the follower's is supererogatory. This could invalidate the congregation. The author suggests a resolution: the immediate understanding (tabadul) from 'following' is doing the like in form and attribute, but the Shari'ah has specified that congregation requires following only in form (surah), not in the ruling of the act. Moreover, in the Hanafi school, the actions of the prayer become obligatory for the follower due to the opening takbir and the act of following, so the requirement of following in both form and attribute is actually fulfilled.

Next, the author examines the verse about the Prophet's marriage to Zaynab bint Jahsh (al-Ahzab:37). The argument is that Allah caused this marriage to occur to remove any difficulty (haraj) for the believers in marrying the wives of their adopted sons. This indicates that the Prophet's action serves as a model (tashrik) for permissibility. However, critics say this only works if the legal aspect (jihah) of the marriage—whether it was obligatory, recommended, or permissible for the Prophet—is known. If unknown, it cannot be used as proof. The author responds that the permissibility of the marriage is clear from the rationale (illah) of negating difficulty, but then engages in a dialectical back-and-forth, ultimately concluding that the marriage was not obligatory for the Prophet but permissible, as indicated by the biography.

The passage then turns to the situation where the ruling of an act is unknown: whether it was obligatory, recommended, or permissible for the Prophet. Three positions are presented: (1) It is obligatory for the Ummah (attributed to Imam Malik and some Hanafis), (2) It is recommended (attributed to al-Shafi'i), and (3) It is permissible (the correct view according to most Hanafis and chosen by Abu Bakr al-Jassas). The author clarifies that the criterion is whether the Prophet consistently persevered (muwazabah) upon the act without ever leaving it. If so, some argue it indicates obligation. But the author critiques this by giving counterexamples: congregational prayer, adhan, iqamah, etc., are all performed consistently yet are sunnah, not obligatory. Thus, mere perseverance is not proof of obligation; a clear sign (qarinah) of intending nearness (qurbah) is needed, and permissible acts lack this.

The proponents of obligation then bring two Quranic proofs: (1) 'Whatever the Messenger gives you, take it' (al-Hashr:7), arguing that the command to 'take' implies obligation; the answer is that it refers to his commands, not his actions. (2) The command 'Follow' (fa-ttabi'u) in Aal-i-Imran:31. The answer is that the command pertains to obligations already known, not to every action, otherwise it would force the conjunction of opposites (obligation and recommendation/permissibility) if the Prophet's act is recommended/permissible. The objectors claim that if the act's aspect is unknown, it must be obligatory for us; if known, it cannot be obligatory. The author rejoins by distinguishing between the act's own ruling (which may be permissible) and the obligation to follow due to the command of following—an obligation arising from an external factor (wujub bil-ghayr). This does not contradict the act's inherent permissibility, just as eating something sworn upon is inherently permissible but becomes obligatory to fulfill the oath.

Finally, a hadith is cited: the Prophet removed his sandals in prayer, and the Companions did the same. When asked why, they said 'You removed, so we removed.' This shows immediate following. The passage ends with 'al-mahfuz ra'aynaka', indicating the narration continues.

The overall Hanafi position is that the Prophet's actions do not automatically impose obligation; they can indicate recommendation or permissibility depending on context, perseverance, and the presence of an intention of nearness. The relied-upon view among the Hanafis is that when the ruling is unknown, the act is permissible for the Ummah, unless there is evidence of obligation. This lesson illustrates the sophisticated usul methodology of the Hanafi school in deriving rulings from the Sunnah.

Key Terms
المواظبة (al-muwazabah): Perseverance upon an act without leaving it.
الإتباع (al-ittiba'): Following the Prophet by doing the like of his action in form and attribute, often synonymous with ta'si.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling for the Ummah when the hukm (obligation, recommendation, permissibility) of a Prophet's action is unknown?
A) It is obligatory (wajib) to perform it exactly as he did.
B) It is recommended (mandub) to perform it.
C) It is permissible (mubah) to perform it.
D) It is prohibited (haram) to perform it without knowledge.

Key Takeaway
When the legal ruling of a Prophet's action is unknown, the Hanafi default is permissibility, not obligation, and consistent performance (muwazabah) does not change that.

Al-Sunnah as Source
intermediate
8 min
Does the Prophet's Action Imply Obligation?
Dispute over fi'l al-rasul and wujub

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on whether the Prophet's action (fi'l) indicates obligation, and apply the usul method to distinguish between mudawamah and tabligh in deriving rulings.

Core Idea
The Prophet's action does not automatically indicate wujub; it may indicate nadb or ibaha unless a qarina of qurba is present.

The Hanafi school holds that the mere action of the Prophet (fi'l) does not necessarily signal obligation. This lesson examines the key dispute over the indicativeness of fi'l, contrasting the Hanafi view with those who argue for wujub. We will analyze the arguments from both sides, focusing on the usul principles of mudawamah (continuity) and tabligh (communication).

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The central question is whether the Prophet's action (fi'l) indicates obligation (wujub) by itself. The Hanafi position is that it does not; it may indicate recommendation (nadb) or permission (ibaha) unless there is contextual evidence (qarina) of seeking closeness to Allah (qurba).
2
Examine the mudawamah (continuity) argument
Some argue that the Prophet's continuous practice (mudawamah) proves obligation. The Hanafi response: many sunnah acts (e.g., congregational prayer, adhan) are performed continuously but are not obligatory. Distinguish between mudawamah and qarina of qurba.
Mudawamah alone is insufficient for wujub.
3
Analyze the five proofs for wujub and their rebuttals
Opponents cite Quran 59:7, 3:31, the hadith of removing sandals, consensus on ghusl, and precaution. For each, the Hanafi response either reinterprets the text or limits its applicability. Work through each pair systematically.
Use the madhhab_dispute_map for reference.
4
Understand the nadb camp's argument and Hanafi refinement
The advocates of recommendation argue that without tabligh (explicit communication), obligation cannot be established; permission is negated because emulation is praised. The Hanafi response: tabligh can be by inference (istinbat), and the action is tabligh for whatever ruling it indicates.
Tabligh is a condition for all rulings, not just wujub.
5
Derive the practical implication (thamarah)
The difference matters in fiqh: if an action of the Prophet is observed, it is not automatically obligatory. The example from the passage is the Companions removing sandals in prayer—it is not obligatory.
The thamarah al-khilaf is that rulings derived from fi'l require additional evidence for wujub.

بنفس رواية الفعل، لكن لا لأنه الموجب بل هو بيان لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِن كُنتُم جُنُبًا فَأَطَهَرُوا ﴾ [المائدة: (٦] لأن الجنابة مجملة، فالتحق هذا الفعل بياناً له (و) قالوا : (خامساً) الإيجاب (أحوط) فيجب القول به الجواب : منع الكلية أي كلية كل ما كان أحوط يجب (بل) إنما هو (فيما ثبت وجوبه من قبل، فيجب فيه ما يخرج به عن العهدة يقيناً كالصلاة المنسية) كما إذا فات صلاة من صلوات يوم فنسيها فيجب عليه قضاء الصلوات الخمس من ذلك اليوم ليخرج عن عهدة المنسية يقيناً، ومنه نسيان المستحاضة أيامها يجب عليها الغسل لكل صلاة (أو كان) الوجوب (هو الأصل) ثم يعرض عليه ما يوجب الشك كصوم (ثلاثين من شهر رمضان، فإن الوجوب فيه الأصل وعروض عارض الغمام لا يمنعه فيجب احتياطاً (لا) كصوم الشك) أي لا يثبت الوجوب للاحتياط في مثل صوم الشك، لأن الوجوب ليس فيه الأصل، ولا هو ثابت يقيناً فافهم فإنه الحق النادبون (قالوا أولاً : ( إذا لم يكن للندب فللوجوب أو الإباحة إذ لا معصية و ( لا وجوب لانتفاء التبليغ الذي هو شرط الوجوب (ولا) إباحة ، لأنه مدح بالتأسي ولا مدح على المباح، والجواب التبليغ أعم صريحاً) كان (أو استنباطاً) والفعل وإن لم يكن تبليغاً صريحاً لكنه تبليغ استنباطاً (وهو يعم (الأحكام) فمن يجعله للوجوب يقول إنه تبليغ الوجوب، ومن جعله للندب أو الإباحة فعنده تبليغ أو الإباحة، ويمكن أن يجعل قوله هذا جواباً آخر تقريره أن التبليغ ليس من شرائط الوجوب فقط، بل هو من لوازم الأحكام كلها، فلو انتفى التبليغ انتفى الندب، فالدليل مقلوب عليكم، فقد ظهر وجه ما في الحاشية» من تتمة الجواب، ويمكن أن يجعل إشارة إلى النقض، ولك أن تقول إن عادته الشريفة كانت أكثر اهتماماً بالواجب، وكان يبين صريحاً ويبالغ فيه، فلو كان الوجوب لبينه صريحاً أو بالغ فيه، وحينئذ اندفع الجوابان فافهم، وأيضاً نختار الإباحة والمدح بالتأسي لا بالمباح والتأسي مندوب، فما مدح عليه فهو
The issue of continuity (mudawamah) on performing an action: it is for obligation according to them, as appears from al-Hidayah, for he argued for the obligation of the two Eid prayers by continuity without omission, but this is not uniform, for the congregational prayer is an emphatic sunnah, yet he never omitted it at all. Similarly, the adhan, iqamah, eclipse prayer, second sermon on Friday, i'tikaf, order and continuity in wudu, and likewise rinsing the mouth and nose and other things for which continuity without omission is established, yet they are sunnah. He himself argued for the sunnah status of most of them by continuity without specifying its omission; rather, non-omission is established. So reflect well and you will learn that continuity is not evidence for obligation according to them. (And) it is also appropriate that this (be) when there is no indication of intending closeness (qurba), since there is no closeness in a permissible act—this is apparent. And this is the chosen view of al-Amidi among the Shafi'is, and al-Waqif, and upon it is Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi among us, and Imam al-Razi, author of al-Mahsul among the Shafi'is, and attributed to most of the Ash'aris. Those who hold obligation said: First: Allah said, 'And whatever the Messenger gives you, take it' (59:7), and the command is for obligation unless there is a diverting evidence, so taking the action is obligatory. The answer: The meaning is 'whatever He commands you, take it' in contrast to 'and whatever He forbids you, abstain.' Second: Allah said, 'So follow' (3:31), and following is doing the like, so the like is obligatory. The answer: The intended following is in beliefs, knowledge, and actions, or following in known obligations, and the verse is not left on its generality, for permissible acts are not obligatory. (And) otherwise, in every estimation, obligation of doing the like of every action leads to two opposites relative to us: obligation and recommendation or permission, if He does it in reality in a way of permission or recommendation, because emulation in the same attribute is necessary, so it would be permissible or recommended, yet you have obligated it in this case, so two opposites are necessitated. An objection was raised: denial that it is permissible on the assumption that its aspect is not known, i.e., if the action done on permission is of unknown aspect, it is not permissible for us, rather it is obligatory. And denial that it is obligatory on the assumption that it is known, meaning if its aspect is known, it is not obligatory. I say: permission and obligation are presumed; as for permission, because he assumed the action is permissible for him, and permission for him entails permission for us in reality; as for obligation, because you said that the action of unknown aspect is obligatory for us. And denial of the presumed is not valid. In it is that the objector's purpose is that unknown permission does not entail permission, and if known, then no obligation, so the two opposites are not necessitated. So permission in the unknown is not presumed, nor obligation in the known is presumed. In the gloss: this commitment is far from fairness. Then similar to this is raised against those who say permission: if the action is in the manner of obligation, then the meeting of two opposites occurs. The answer is that the action in the manner of obligation with lack of knowledge of it is not permissible, for the noble custom is to increase care for the obligatory, its clarification and emphasis. Yes, it may be objected that obligation by another (i.e., following) does not negate permission in itself. Meaning we accept the meeting of permission and obligation, but permission with respect to the action itself, and obligation because of following, so no problem, like eating that is sworn upon: it is permissible in itself, but because of fulfilling the oath it is obligatory. Consider. Third: The Messenger of Allah removed his sandals in prayer, so they began to remove their sandals. He said, 'What made you throw your sandals?' They said, 'You removed, so we removed.' The preserved version is: 'We saw you throw, so we threw.' He said, 'Jibril came to me and informed me that there was something harmful in them.' Reported by Ahmad. So he affirmed them in following and did not disapprove of the following itself, and he specified his own case with Jibril's news that there was harm in his sandal, indicating that following is obligatory. The answer: It does not indicate obligation of following; rather, the utmost of it is that they followed him, and it is possible it was due to thinking it recommended or choosing one side of the permissible. And even if obligation is conceded, and that they followed due to their belief of obligation, then they understood from his saying 'Take from me'—i.e., even if understanding obligation is conceded, they understood it from his saying 'Take from me,' and they assumed it was a prohibition of praying in sandals, as in other pillars of prayer. In the gloss: This hadith is established by many chains and by religious necessity, and it is more general than 'Pray as you see me pray,' so what is in al-Tahrir that he did not say it later is refuted, and his saying at the revelation of the hadd of zina is already passed. Fourth: They disagreed over the obligation of ghusl for penetration without ejaculation, then they agreed upon it due to the narration of Mother of the Believers Aisha that he did it, and its extraction has passed. So if the action were not for obligation, they would not have agreed due to knowing the action. The answer: We do not concede that they agreed due to the action itself, but rather due to his saying 'When the circumcised part touches the circumcised part, ghusl becomes obligatory' reported by the two shaykhs. Or we say: We concede they agreed due to the narration of the action, but not because it obligates, rather it is a clarification of His saying 'And if you are in a state of janaba, then purify yourselves' (5:6), because janaba is ambiguous, so this action became a clarification of it. Fifth: Obligation is more precautionary, so one must say it. The answer: Denial of the generality that everything precautionary is obligatory; rather, it is only in what is previously established as obligatory, where one must do what definitely clears one's responsibility, like the forgotten prayer: if a prayer of a day is missed and one forgets it, one must pray all five prayers of that day to clear one's responsibility definitely, and similarly, the forgetfulness of a mustahada regarding her days necessitates ghusl for every prayer. Or if obligation is the original state and then something arises that causes doubt, like fasting thirty days of Ramadan: obligation is the original, and the occurrence of a cloud does not prevent it, so it must be done precautionarily. Not like the fast of doubt: obligation is not established by precaution in such cases, because obligation is not the original nor is it definitely established. So understand, for it is the truth. Those who hold recommendation said: First: If it is not for recommendation, then it must be for obligation or permission, since there is no disobedience. And there is no obligation due to lack of tabligh (communication) which is a condition of obligation, and no permission because one is praised for emulation, and there is no praise for permissible acts. The answer: Tabligh is general, whether explicit or inferred; the action, though not explicit tabligh, is tabligh by inference. And it encompasses all rulings. So whoever makes it for obligation says it is tabligh of obligation, and whoever makes it for recommendation or permission holds it is tabligh of that. And this can be made another answer: tabligh is not only a condition for obligation but is a necessary concomitant of all rulings; if tabligh were absent, recommendation would be absent, so the proof is reversed against you. So the point of what is in the gloss as a supplement to the answer has become clear. And it can be made a reference to refutation. And you may say that his noble custom was more caring for the obligatory, and he would clarify it explicitly and emphasize it, so if it were obligatory, he would have clarified it explicitly or emphasized it. Then both answers are refuted. Consider. Also we choose permission, and the praise is for emulation, not for the permissible, and emulation is recommended, so what is praised for it is...
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed usul dispute about whether the Prophet's action (fi'l) indicates obligation (wujub). It is from a Hanafi commentary on Musallam al-Thubut. The author first refutes the idea that continuous practice (mudawamah) proves obligation, giving counterexamples like congregational prayer, adhan, etc., which are sunnah despite continuous practice. He then states the relied-upon position: the Prophet's action indicates something other than obligation (nadb or ibaha) unless there is a qarina indicating qurba. This is the view of al-Amidi, al-Karkhi, and al-Razi. The rest of the passage presents five arguments from those who claim wujub and the Hanafi responses. First, the verse 'Take what the Messenger gives you' is answered by specifying it refers to commands, not all actions. Second, the verse 'Follow' is answered by limiting it to obligations already known; otherwise it would lead to contradictions. Third, the hadith of the Prophet removing sandals in prayer is answered: the Companions understood obligation from his general command 'Take from me,' not from the act itself. Fourth, the consensus on ghusl for penetration is traced back to the Prophet's explicit statement, not his action. Fifth, precaution (ihtiyat) only applies where obligation is already established; it cannot create new obligations. Finally, the nadb camp's argument is presented: if the action is not for nadb, it must be for wujub or ibaha; but wujub is negated by lack of explicit tabligh, and ibaha is negated by praise for emulation. The Hanafi response is that tabligh is not only for wujub but for all rulings via inference; the Prophet's action can be tabligh for any ruling. The author also notes a possible refutation: the Prophet's practice was to emphasize obligations explicitly, so lack of explicit statement suggests non-obligation.

Key Terms
الفعل (al-fi'l): The action of the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم as a source of legislation (fi'l al-rasul).
المداومة (al-mudawamah): Continuity of practice; the Prophet's continuous performance of an act without omission.
التبليغ (al-tabligh): Communication of the ruling; the act of conveying the legal rule.
القرينة (al-qarinah): An indicator or contextual evidence that specifies the intended ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is the primary reason the Hanafi school rejects the argument that the Prophet's action indicates obligation?
A) Because the Quran abrogates all Prophetic actions.
B) Because many sunnah acts are performed continuously without being obligatory.
C) Because the Prophet's actions are only for the Companions, not later generations.
D) Because the hadith of sandals explicitly forbids following.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's action (fi'l) does not automatically indicate obligation; it indicates nadb or ibaha unless there is a qarina of seeking closeness.

Al-Sunnah as Source
intermediate
8 min
The Evidential Value of the Prophet's Actions (Af'al)
Debate on whether Prophet's actions indicate obligation, recommendation, or permissibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three main positions on the evidential value of the Prophet's actions and apply the Hanafi default of permissibility when no indication of closeness is present.

Core Idea
Prophet's actions default to permissibility unless a clear indication of closeness suggests a higher ruling.

When the Prophet performed an action, does that make it obligatory, recommended, or merely permissible for us? The scholars differed on this foundational usul issue. In this passage, we examine the main arguments for each position and see how the Hanafi school arrives at its default of permissibility (ibaha) for actions lacking a clear devotional context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
The passage discusses three views: obligation (wujub), recommendation (nadb), and permissibility (ibaha). Some also suspended judgment (tawaqquf). First, clearly state each position and its proponents.
2
Trace the proof for obligation
The proponents of obligation cite the hadith 'Take from me' and the principle of precaution. Follow how they argue that the Prophet's actions must be followed as obligations.
3
Analyze the proof for recommendation
The proponents of recommendation argue that most of the Prophet's actions were devotional and he is praised for following (ta'assi). Understand how they derive nadb from the general practice.
4
Understand the Hanafi proof for permissibility
The majority Hanafis argue that in the absence of any indication (qarina) of closeness (qurba), the action is merely permissible. Their proof: the Prophet's habit of seeking ease for the ummah, the negation of haram and khassiyya, and the absence of additional command.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: For a Hanafi, an action of the Prophet not clearly devotional is mubah — no sin for leaving, no reward for doing. This differs from those who deem it wajib or mandub. Apply this to a real example.

بالقرب وجوابه أن عند عدم ظهور القربة الغالب المباح كما يظهر لمن تتبع أحواله الشريفة حرصاً على التسهيل على الأمة . أكثر الحنفية قالوا : الإباحة هو المتيقن عند عدم قرينة القربة لأنه لو لم يكن مأذوناً فيه لكان حراماً عليه وعلينا أو مختصاً به وهما منتفيان الانتفاء المعصية والخصوصية) وأقل مراتب المأذون الإباحة لعدم الأمر الزائد المفروض إنه لا قربة، فلا وجوب ولا ندب وذلك لأن الأحكام فيما لم يظهر قصد القربة، فلو كان لبينه لكثرة الاهتمام بالقرب فتعين الإباحة، وعلى هذا اندفع ما قال قد يقال : إنما يتم هذا الوجه (لو كان المدعي الإمكان العام وهو مطلق الإذن أعم من أن يكون مع الحرج في الترك أم لا (لا الإمكان الخاص وهو الإذن في الفعل مع الإذن في الترك، لكن مدعاهم هو الثاني كيف لا ( وقد نفوا الزائد) على الإذن من الحرج في الترك أو استحقاق المثوبة بالفعل مع عدم الحرج في الترك امتيازاً عن الواقفية ولو لم ينفوا لكان قولهم بالتوقف (اللهم إلا أن يثبتوا جواز الترك (بالأصل فافهم المتوقفون (قالوا الفعل ( يحتمل الخصوص) بالنبي صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم (والعموم) للأمة منتهياً إلى أنواع شتى من الوجوب والندب والإباحة (فالحكم) بأحدها على الأمة (تحكم) فوجب التوقف (الجواب) الاحتمالات وإن كانت كثيرة لكن وضع النبوة للاقتداء وهو دليل الحكم فلا بد من حكم معين على مشارب مختلفة) فمن يرى الوجوب فعنده دليل الوجوب ومن يرى الندب فعنده دليل الندب ومن يرى الإباحة فعنده دليل الإباحة وهو الصحيح (فتدبر).
The second principle/ Sunnah: He threw and we threw. He said: Jibril came to me and informed me that there was harm in it, narrated by Ahmad. (He affirmed them) in following and did not disapprove of the following itself, and he specified by the news of Jibril that there was harm in his sandal, so it indicates that following is obligatory. (The response): It does not indicate obligation; the utmost is that they followed him, and it is possible that it was due to considering it recommended or choosing one side of the permissible. Even if obligation is conceded, they followed him thinking it obligatory due to his saying: 'Take from me.' So they claimed that he forbade praying in sandals, as in other pillars. In the 'Hashiya': This hadith is established by many chains, and it is established by religious necessity, and it is more general than 'Pray as you see me praying.' So what is in 'Al-Tahrir' is refuted that he did not say it after... and his saying was established at the revelation of the hadd of zina as already mentioned. (And) they said: (Fourthly: They disagreed on the obligation of ghusl and insertion without ejaculation, then they agreed on it due to the narration of the Mother of the Believers Aisha al-Siddiqa: his action, and its takhrij has passed. So if the action were not for obligation, they would not have agreed by knowing the action. (Response): We do not concede that they agreed by the action itself, but by his saying: 'When the circumcised part passes the circumcised part, ghusl becomes obligatory,' narrated by the two Shaykhs. (Or) we say: We agree that they agreed by the narration of the action, but not because it is the obligator; rather it is a clarification of His saying: 'If you are junub, then purify yourselves' (al-Ma'idah 6), because janaba is mujmal, so this action becomes a clarification for it. (And) they said: (Fifthly: Obligation is precautionary, so it must be accepted. (Response): Denial of the universal, i.e., every precautionary thing is obligatory; rather it is in what its obligation is previously established, so what removes liability with certainty is obligatory, like the forgotten prayer: if a prayer from the day's prayers is missed and forgotten, he must make up the five prayers of that day to remove liability for the forgotten one with certainty. From it is the mustahada's forgetting her days: she must perform ghusl for each prayer. (Or) obligation is the original, then something arises that causes doubt, like fasting thirty days of Ramadan: obligation is the original and the occurrence of cloud does not prevent it, so it must be done precautionarily. Not like fast of doubt (yawm al-shakk): obligation is not established precautionarily in such fast, because obligation is not the original and it is not certainly established; understand, for it is the truth. The proponents of recommendation (nadibun) said: (First: If the action is not for recommendation, then it is for obligation or permissibility, since there is no disobedience and no obligation due to absence of tabligh which is a condition of obligation, and no permissibility because he is praised for following (ta'assi) and there is no praise for permissible things. (Response): Tabligh is general, explicit or by deduction, and the action, though not explicit tabligh, is tabligh by deduction, and it covers all rulings. So one who makes it for obligation says it is tabligh of obligation, and one who makes it for recommendation or permissibility considers it tabligh of recommendation or permissibility. This response can be made another answer: tabligh is not a condition of obligation only, but a necessary consequence of all rulings; if tabligh is absent, recommendation is also absent, so the proof is turned against you. The point of what is in the 'Hashiya' has become clear as a completion of the response. It can be taken as reference to refutation. You may say that his noble habit was more concerned with obligation, and he would clarify explicitly and emphasize; so if it were obligation, he would have clarified explicitly or emphasized. Then the two answers are refuted; understand. Also we choose permissibility: praise is for following, not for permissible actions, and following is recommended, so what is praised is recommended, and what is permissible is not praised. (And) they said: (Second: The predominant in his actions is recommendation, so it is interpreted as such and becomes recommended as well, for following. (Response): We do not concede predominance of recommended acts; rather the predominant is permissible. I say: For non-natural actions, the apparent is otherwise because most of his concern was with devotional acts. The response is that when there is no appearance of closeness (qurba), the predominant is permissible, as appears to one who traces his noble states, due to eagerness for ease for the ummah. Most Hanafis said: Permissibility is the certain thing when there is no indication of closeness, because if it were not permitted, it would be haram for him and us or specific to him, both of which are negated (negation of disobedience and specificity). The lowest level of permission is permissibility due to the absence of an additional command (assuming no closeness), so no obligation or recommendation. This is because rulings in what does not show intention of closeness: if it were (obligation/recommendation), he would have clarified due to great concern for closeness; so permissibility is determined. Based on this, what was said is refuted: 'This argument only holds if the claimant claims general possibility (al-imkan al-'amm), which is absolute permission whether with difficulty in leaving or not, not specific possibility (al-imkan al-khass), which is permission to do and permission to leave. But their claim is the second.' How not? They have negated anything additional to permission, i.e., difficulty in leaving or deserving reward for doing without difficulty in leaving, distinguishing them from the waqfiyya. If they had not negated, they would be saying suspension. Unless they establish permissibility of leaving by the original. Understand. The suspendors (mutawaqqifun) said: (The action) may be specific to the Prophet or general to the ummah, leading to various types of obligation, recommendation, and permissibility; so judging one of them for the ummah is arbitrary, so suspension is necessary. (Response): The possibilities, though many, the status of prophethood is for following, and that is the evidence of the ruling; so there must be a definite ruling according to different perspectives: one who sees obligation has evidence for obligation, one who sees recommendation has evidence for recommendation, and one who sees permissibility has evidence for permissibility, and it is correct. (Ponder).
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute on the evidential value of the Prophet's actions (af'al). The text presents multiple arguments from those who claim the Prophet's actions indicate obligation (wujub), and from those who claim they indicate recommendation (nadb) or permissibility (ibaha). The Hanafi position, as presented, is that in the absence of qurba (closeness), the default is ibaha (permissibility). The arguments include the hadith on removing sandals, the issue of ghusl, precaution (ihtiyat), the concept of tabligh, and the principle of ta'assi. The text resolves by stating that the Prophet's role is for us to follow, and each mujtahid has his own evidence. The relied-upon position is that actions of the Prophet are for ibaha unless there is a clear indication of higher ruling.

Key Terms
فعل (fi'l): An action of the Prophet, distinct from his qawl (speech) and taqrir (tacit approval).
الوجوب (al-wujub): The ruling of obligation; a demand for action that is binding and punishable if omitted.
الندب (al-nadb): Recommendation; an action that is praised but not obligatory.
الإباحة (al-ibaha): Permissibility; an action that may be done or left without praise or blame.
القرينة (al-qarina): An indication or context that clarifies the legal implication of an action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafis, what is the ruling of a Prophet's action in the absence of any indication of closeness to Allah (qurba)?
A) Obligatory (wajib)
B) Recommended (mandub)
C) Permissible (mubah)
D) Suspended (tawaqquf)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi default for the Prophet's actions is permissibility (ibaha) unless there is a clear indication of closeness (qurba), avoiding unnecessary obligations on the ummah.

Taqrir (Tacit Approval) as Evidence of Permissibility
Discussion on whether the Prophet's silence indicates permissibility of an action

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that taqrir indicates permissibility and distinguish it from original permissibility.

Core Idea
The Prophet's silence on an action only affirms the absence of prohibition, not a positive ruling.

In this passage, the author of Fawatih al-Rahamut discusses the evidentiary weight of the Prophet's tacit approval (taqrir). You will learn the Hanafi position that taqrir indicates permissibility only in the sense of original permissibility, not a new legal command. The text also addresses a key objection about delaying clarification and notes the practical difficulty of determining whether silence was due to approval or ignorance of the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling of taqrir
The passage states that when the Prophet witnesses an action by a Muslim and remains silent while able to disapprove, this indicates permissibility (jawaz) absolutely. Note that 'able to disapprove' is a key condition.
Taqrir is a type of sunnah in Hanafi usul, but weaker than explicit speech.
2
Recognize the objection
Some scholars argue that silence does not indicate permissibility at all, because if it did, it would necessitate delaying clarification of a prohibition or affirming a prohibited act, both incompatible with prophethood.
The objection is based on the principle that the Prophet must clarify rulings when needed.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The author responds that the action may have occurred before its ruling was revealed (especially in early Islam). In that case, silence does not imply approval of the prohibited because there was no prohibition yet, and no clarification is needed. Thus, permissibility from taqrir is only in the sense of absence of prohibition (ibaha asliyya), not a positive ruling.
This distinction prevents taqrir from establishing obligations or recommendations.
4
Apply the distinction
Because taqrir only affirms original permissibility, it cannot be used to derive positive legal consequences like establishing lineage. The text says deriving such rulings from taqrir is 'subject to consideration' and not valid.
This limits the scope of taqrir as a source of law.
5
Note the stronger evidence
If the Prophet showed pleasure (istibshar) with the action, that is clearer evidence of permissibility than mere silence, but still only indicates jawaz.
Pleasure is a form of qawl (speech) or fi'l (action) that strengthens the indication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : إذا علم عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه الصلاة و السلام (الفعل) من أحد (والفاعل غير كافر بل ممن يقبل حكمه بالقلب واللسان فسكت قادراً على إنكاره) يعني لم يكن مانع من أشغال أهم وغيرها وهذا هو التقرير (دل على الجواز مطلقاً) فاعله وقد مر وجه التعميم وقيل : لا يدل أصلاً ( وإلا) يدل على الجواز لزم تأخير البيان) عن وقت الحاجة (وتقرير المحرم مع القدرة على الإنكار، وشأن النبوة بريء عنها (أقول: يجوز أن يكون ذلك الفعل ( مما لم يعلم حكمه بعد، سيما في بدء الإسلام وحينئذ لا يلزم من ترك الإنكار تأخير البيان لعدم ما يلزم بيانه ولا تقرير المحرم لعدم التحريم (فلا جواز) أي فلا يثبت الجواز (إلا بمعنى عدم الحرام) كما هو شأن الإباحة الأصلية، وحينئذ (فتفريع الأحكام الشرعية كإثبات النسب شرعاً عليه محل نظر) فإنه لا يثبت به حكم شرعي فافهم وقد قال بعضهم في عنوان المسألة : إذا سكت ولم يكن حامل السكوت كعدم معرفة الحكم وعلى هذا يرد عليه هذا الإيراد لكن معرفة أنه سكوت مع معرفة الحكم أو بدونها عسير إلا إذا علم مخالف بلا عمل به فافهم وإن استبشر به أي بالفعل مع التقرير (فأوضح) دلالة على الجواز وهو ظاهر .
Issue: If the Prophet (peace and blessings upon him and his family and companions) knew of an action performed by someone, and the doer is not a disbeliever but one whose judgment is accepted by heart and tongue, and he remained silent while able to disapprove, meaning there was no hindrance such as more important occupations, and this is taqrir (tacit approval), it indicates permissibility absolutely. The reason for generalization has been mentioned. And some say: it does not indicate at all. Otherwise, if it indicates permissibility, it would necessitate delaying the clarification from the time of need and approving the forbidden while able to disapprove, and the nature of prophethood is free from that. I say: It is possible that the action was not yet known in its ruling, especially in early Islam, and then not disapproving does not necessitate delaying clarification because there is nothing to clarify, nor approving the forbidden because there is no prohibition. So permissibility is not established except in the sense of absence of prohibition, as is the case with original permissibility. Then deriving legal rulings from it, such as establishing lineage legally, is subject to consideration; for it does not establish a legal ruling. Understand. Some have said in the title of the issue: If he was silent and the reason for silence is not known, like not knowing the ruling, then this objection applies. But knowing whether his silence was with knowledge of the ruling or without is difficult unless one knows an opposing person who does not act upon it. Understand. And if he was pleased with it, i.e., the action with taqrir, then it is clearer in indicating permissibility, and that is apparent.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage discusses the principle of taqrir (tacit approval) in Hanafi usul. The rule is that when the Prophet witnessed an action performed by a Muslim who is subject to Islamic rulings, and he remained silent despite being able to disapprove, this indicates that the action is permissible (ja'iz). This is because if it were haram or makruh, he would have disapproved. However, some scholars argue that silence does not necessarily indicate permissibility, especially if the ruling was not yet revealed. The text offers a response: In early Islam, some actions were not yet legislated, so silence does not imply approval of a prohibition. Thus, taqrir only establishes permissibility in the sense of absence of prohibition (ibaha asliyya), not a positive legal ruling. Therefore, one cannot derive specific legal consequences like establishing lineage from mere taqrir. The passage concludes that if the Prophet showed pleasure, that is stronger evidence of permissibility.

Key Terms
التقرير (al-taqrir): Tacit approval; the Prophet's silence on an action done in his presence, indicating permissibility.
الجواز (al-jawaz): Permissibility; the ruling that an action may be done without punishment or reward.
الإباحة الأصلية (al-ibaha al-asliyya): Original permissibility; the default ruling that things are permissible in the absence of a revealed prohibition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary implication of the Prophet's taqrir (tacit approval) of an action?
A) The action is obligatory
B) The action is recommended
C) The action is permissible (absence of prohibition)
D) The action is prohibited

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's taqrir (silent approval) indicates only that the action is not prohibited, not that it is a positive command or recommendation.

The Indicative Value of Taqrir (Tacit Approval) for Permissibility
Stating the rule that silence indicates permissibility, with rebuttal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the evidentiary value of taqrir and distinguish when it indicates permissibility versus when it only affirms original ibahah.

Core Idea
Taqrir indicates permissibility only when the Prophet knew the ruling; otherwise, it merely affirms original non-prohibition.

The Prophet's silence (taqrir) is a source of Sunnah in Hanafi usul. But not all silences are equal. This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut explores the conditions under which silence indicates permissibility, and when it does not. Understanding this nuance is essential before deriving rulings from the Prophet's tacit approvals.

Guided Path
1
Define taqrir
Identify the definition: the Prophet's silence when witnessing an action, while able to object, with no impediment.
Taqrir is a type of Sunnah.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The Hanafi position is that such silence indicates permissibility (jawaz) for the doer and others.
3
Identify the condition
The Prophet must know the ruling of the action at that time and be capable of objecting. If the ruling was unknown, silence does not indicate jawaz beyond original ibahah.
4
Analyze the counterargument
Some argue that silence may occur because the ruling was not yet revealed, so it does not prove permissibility. The Hanafi response is that the default is that the Prophet knew the ruling.
5
Evaluate the practical consequence
If silence indicates permissibility, it can be used as a precedent. If not, it only confirms absence of prohibition and cannot establish new rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : إذا علم عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه الصلاة و السلام (الفعل) من أحد (والفاعل غير كافر بل ممن يقبل حكمه بالقلب واللسان فسكت قادراً على إنكاره) يعني لم يكن مانع من أشغال أهم وغيرها وهذا هو التقرير (دل على الجواز مطلقاً) فاعله وقد مر وجه التعميم وقيل : لا يدل أصلاً ( وإلا) يدل على الجواز لزم تأخير البيان) عن وقت الحاجة (وتقرير المحرم مع القدرة على الإنكار، وشأن النبوة بريء عنها (أقول: يجوز أن يكون ذلك الفعل ( مما لم يعلم حكمه بعد، سيما في بدء الإسلام وحينئذ لا يلزم من ترك الإنكار تأخير البيان لعدم ما يلزم بيانه ولا تقرير المحرم لعدم التحريم (فلا جواز) أي فلا يثبت الجواز (إلا بمعنى عدم الحرام) كما هو شأن الإباحة الأصلية، وحينئذ (فتفريع الأحكام الشرعية كإثبات النسب شرعاً عليه محل نظر) فإنه لا يثبت به حكم شرعي فافهم وقد قال بعضهم في عنوان المسألة : إذا سكت ولم يكن حامل السكوت كعدم معرفة الحكم وعلى هذا يرد عليه هذا الإيراد لكن معرفة أنه سكوت مع معرفة الحكم أو بدونها عسير إلا إذا علم مخالف بلا عمل به فافهم وإن استبشر به أي بالفعل مع التقرير (فأوضح) دلالة على لا حكم الجواز وهو ظاهر .
Issue: If the action (was known) from someone, and the doer is not a disbeliever but rather someone whose judgment is accepted by heart and tongue, then he (the Prophet) remained silent while being able to object, meaning there was no obstacle of more important engagements etc., and that is tacit approval; it indicates permissibility absolutely for the doer and others. And the aspect of generality was mentioned earlier. And it is said: it does not indicate at all. Otherwise, if it indicated permissibility, it would necessitate delay of clarification beyond the time of need, and approving the forbidden while able to object, and the status of prophethood is free from these. I say: It is possible that that action was one whose ruling was not known yet, especially in early Islam, and then there is no necessity of delay of clarification because there is no need to clarify, and no approval of the forbidden because there is no prohibition. So it does not indicate permissibility, except in the sense of absence of prohibition, which is the nature of original permissibility. And then deriving legal rulings from it, such as proving lineage by it, is questionable because a legal ruling cannot be established by it. And some have said in the title of the issue: If he remained silent and the factor behind silence was not known, like not knowing the ruling, then this objection applies. But knowing whether the silence was with knowledge of the ruling or without is difficult, unless one knows an opponent who does not act by it. So understand. And if he showed joy along with the action and the approval, then it is more clear in indicating permissibility, and that is apparent.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage addresses the principle of taqrir (tacit approval) as a source of legislation. It defines taqrir as the Prophet's silence when witnessing an action performed by someone subject to his rulings, while he was capable of objecting, without any impediment. According to the majority, this silence indicates the permissibility of that action for both the doer and others. The reason is that if the action were not permissible, the Prophet would have objected; his silence would then imply approval. However, there is a counterargument: if the action's ruling was not yet known (especially in early Islam), then silence does not necessarily indicate permissibility; it only confirms the absence of prohibition (original ibahah). In that case, no legal ruling such as lineage can be derived from it. The passage also notes that if the Prophet showed joy (istibshar) along with silence, it provides a clearer indication of permissibility. The author (likely 'Allama al-Anbabi or another commentator) supports the view that when the action's ruling is known, silence indicates permissibility; but when unknown, it only affirms the default of non-prohibition. This nuance is important for deriving further rulings from the Prophet's actions.

Key Terms
التقرير (al-taqrir): The Prophet's silence when witnessing an action, indicating tacit approval.
الإنكار (al-inkar): Verbal or physical objection to a forbidden action.
الإباحة الأصلية (al-ibahah al-asliyyah): Original permissibility; the default ruling that something is permitted until evidence of prohibition appears.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition does the Prophet's silence (taqrir) indicate permissibility?
A) Always
B) Only if the action's ruling was known to him at that time
C) Only if the doer is a believer
D) If the majority of his actions are mandub

Key Takeaway
Taqrir indicates permissibility only when the Prophet knew the ruling and was capable of objecting; otherwise, it only affirms the original absence of prohibition.

Authority of a Companion's Opinion (Qawl al-Sahabi) in Hanafi Usul
Listing of scholars who accept the companion's opinion as authority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the prominent scholars who accept qawl al-sahabi as a source and explain the condition "fima yumkinu fih al-ra'y".

Core Idea
The Companion's opinion is a contested source: accepted by Hanafis, Malik, Shafi'i (old), and Ahmad (one narration), but not universally.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut introduces a key mas'ala in usul al-fiqh: the authority of a Companion's legal opinion (qawl al-sahabi). While some schools accept it as a binding source in matters of ijtihad, others reject it. The text lists the major supporters, including leading Hanafi authorities and Imams from other schools, setting the stage for the debate that follows.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
What is qawl al-sahabi? It is the legal opinion of a Companion of the Prophet. The passage discusses its authority in matters that can be reached by ijtihad ("fima yumkinu fih al-ra'y").
This excludes purely transmitted matters (e.g., tafsir of Quran without ijtihad).
2
List the Hanafi scholars
Find and record the names: Abu Bakr al-Razi, Abu Sa'id al-Barda'i, Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi, Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi. Note their titles and lineages.
These are key early Hanafi usul authorities.
3
List the non-Hanafi scholars
Find: Imam Malik, Imam al-Shafi'i (in his old school, qadim), and Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal (in one narration).
The mention of Shafi'i's old view indicates he later changed his position.
4
Understand the limitation
The phrase "fima yumkinu fih al-ra'y" limits the authority to matters where personal reasoning (ra'y) is applicable, not to definitive texts or matters of consensus.
This excludes matters like the number of prayers or clear Quranic rulings.
5
Recognize the significance
By listing these authorities, the passage establishes that qawl al-sahabi is a recognized source (hujja) for many scholars, placing it alongside qiyas. This sets the foundation for the later arguments.
The differing positions will be explored in subsequent lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(مسألة) قال الشيخ أبو بكر الرازي منا و الإمام أبو سعيد (البردعي) بكسر الباء الموحدة وفتح الدال والعين المهملة منسوب إلى بردعة من أقصى بلاد أذربيجان (و) الإمام فخر الإسلام البزدوي و) شمس الأئمة (السرخسي) قدس أسرارهم (وأتباعهم مالك والشافعي في القول (القديم وأحمد في رواية رحمهم الله تعالى : قول الصحابي فيما يمكن فيه الرأي
(Issue) Al-Shaykh Abu Bakr al-Razi among us, and al-Imam Abu Sa'id al-Barda'i (with kasra on the ba and fatha on the dal and 'ayn, attributed to Bard'a, a distant region of Azerbaijan), and Imam Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi, and Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi (may their secrets be sanctified), and their followers: Malik, al-Shafi'i in the old view, and Ahmad in one narration (may Allah have mercy on them): The saying of the Companion in matters subject to opinion [is authoritative].
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage opens a new mas'ala on the legal authority of a Companion's opinion (qawl al-sahabi). The text lists the scholars who maintain that the saying of a Companion is a valid source of law in matters that can be reached by ijtihad (ويمكن فيه الرأي). Notably, the proponents include major Hanafi authorities: Abu Bakr al-Razi (likely the Hanafi jurist, not to be confused with the philosopher), Abu Sa'id al-Barda'i, Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi, and Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi. It also includes Malik, al-Shafi'i (in his old school), and Ahmad ibn Hanbal (in one narration). The passage does not yet provide the argument or counterarguments; it simply sets up the position that qawl al-sahabi is a source.

Key Terms
قول الصحابي (qawl al-sahabi): The legal opinion of a Companion of the Prophet, considered by many scholars as a valid source of law when analogical reasoning is possible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Companion's opinion is a recognised source of law for many scholars, but its authority is limited to matters of juristic reasoning and is not universally accepted.

Hujjiyyat Qawl al-Sahabi: The Bindingness of the Companion's Opinion
Debate on bindingness of Companion's opinion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on qawl al-Sahabi, explain the illah (presumption of hearing) for its bindingness, and evaluate the denier's objections using the Hanafi rebuttals.

Core Idea
The Companion's opinion binds later jurists because of the presumption he heard from the Prophet, not mere knowledge.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut engages a classic usul dispute: Is the opinion of a Companion (qawl al-Sahabi) binding evidence for later mujtahids? The Hanafi school holds that it is annexed to the Sunnah (mulhaq bi al-sunnah), meaning it must be followed. Other schools, like the new view of al-Shafi'i, treat it as a non-binding opinion. This lesson dissects the arguments, the underlying illah, and the Hanafi response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the positions
List the scholars who affirm that qawl al-Sahabi is binding for non-Companions, and those who deny it. Note the nuanced view that only Abu Bakr and 'Umar might be binding.
The text names Hanafi authorities (al-Razi, al-Barda'i, etc.), Malik, Shafi'i in old view, Ahmad in one narration. Deniers: Shafi'i new, al-Karkhi.
2
Locate the illah (cause)
Find the exact phrase that explains why the Companion's opinion is considered binding. The Hanafis state the illah is 'zann al-sama' (presumption of hearing) from the Lawgiver and understanding his intent through witnessing circumstances.
Key phrase: 'العلة ظن السماع' (the cause is the presumption of hearing).
3
Analyze the first objection and response
The denier argues: if binding due to knowledge/virtue, then any knowledgeable non-Companion would also bind (false by consensus). The Hanafi response distinguishes: the illah is not general knowledge but the specific assumption of direct hearing from the Prophet.
The Hanafi rebuttal: 'لا نسلم أنه لا يصلح للعلية إلا الأعلمية والأفضلية' (we do not accept that the cause is only knowledge and virtue).
4
Examine the second objection (contradictions) and response
The denier claims bindingness would lead to contradictory obligations since Companions differ. The Hanafi response: there is no abrogation after the Prophet, but truth is one; we resolve through tarjih (preponderance), takhyir (choice), or tawaquf (suspension).
Analogous to khabar al-wahid contradictions, but without naskh.
5
Note scope limitations
The passage restricts bindingness to senior Companions (e.g., caliphs, 'Abadilah), not later converts. Also excludes matters of 'amma al-balwa (widespread issues) where Companion opinion contradicts public practice.
This prevents overgeneralization of the Hanafi position.

بالتاريخ تعارضا عندنا بخلاف ما نحن فيه، إذ لا نسخ بعد وفاته صلوات الله وسلامه عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه والجواب أنه لا تناقض ههنا أيضاً لأن إصابة الحق كان أكثرياً فإذا تخلف فالحق أحدهما في نفس الأمر، لكن لجهلنا به وعدم الأولوية وقع التعارض ظاهراً فلا يلزم التناقض (بل) اللازم الترجيح بالرأي أو التخيير) في العمل (أو التوقف) ويعمل بالقياس أو الأصل على اختلاف القولين كما سيجيء (و) قال النافي (ثالثاً) لو Issue: Shaykh Abu Bakr al-Razi from us, and Imam Abu Sa'id al-Barda'i (with kasr of ba, fatha of dal and 'ayn, attributed to Bardha'a, a far region of Azerbaijan), and Imam Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi, and Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi (may their secrets be sanctified), and their followers: Malik, al-Shafi'i in the old opinion, and Ahmad in one narration (may Allah have mercy on them) hold that the saying of a Companion in matters where personal opinion is possible is annexed to the Sunnah for others (i.e., for non-Companions) so it is obligatory to follow his opinion and abandon one's own. (Not for one like him) meaning it is not annexed to the Sunnah regarding another Companion, so he is not obliged to follow him. Al-Shafi'i denied this in the new opinion, and Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi from us and a group (held similarly), and on this the followers of al-Shafi'i continued, saying his saying and the saying of another mujtahid are equal. And it is said: the saying of the two shaykhs, the two imams, the Commander of the Faithfuls, Abu Bakr and 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with them) is annexed to the Sunnah only, not the sayings of other Companions. And the dispute should be about those Companions who spent their lives in companionship and acquired his noble character, like the caliphs, the purified wives, the 'Abadilah, Anas, Hudhayfah, and their class, not the ones of the conquest of Mecca, for most of them did not acquire knowledge of legal rulings except by taqlid, and Allah knows best. And the dispute is in matters that are not widespread (ma lam ya'm balwa); as for matters that are widespread (amma 'amma al-balwa) and a Companion's saying contradicts the practice of those afflicted, it is not obligatory to take it, by agreement, because the Sunnah is not accepted in such matters, so that which is subject to doubt is not accepted. And if the Companions differ among themselves, then it is not obligatory to take any; rather, reflection is needed, and what agrees with one's opinion is taken, unless the others remained silent knowing of it, for then it is ijma' which must be followed by agreement. The denier said: first, if his opinion were a proof, then the saying of any more knowledgeable and virtuous (non-Companion) would also be a proof, and the consequent is false by consensus, for it is not suitable as a cause (being a proof) except for being such, i.e., more knowledgeable and superior, since there is no infallibility. I say: we do not accept that it is suitable only for knowledge and superiority; rather, the cause is the presumption of hearing from the Lawgiver and understanding his intent through witnessing the circumstances, given their noble habit of giving fatwa based on text except rarely, and presumption follows the majority. So understand. And know that on this basis, one should not follow his opinion if he explicitly says he gave fatwa based on opinion, and the phrasing of Imam Fakhr al-Islam rejects this. And the answer given in the commentary that the Companions may have an influence on hujjiyyah, so the cause is companionship, so it does not entail the opinion of every more knowledgeable and superior being proof. So I say: it is repelled by the fact that there is no ruling except the ruling of the Shari', so companionship has no entry in hujjiyyah. So reflect. And you can formulate the answer that the blessing of companionship and acquiring prophetic character creates a presumption of correctness and lack of error in their opinion, so their opinion is a proof because it is truth conforming to the ruling with Allah. And this is not far, for such blessings lead to what no eye has seen, no ear has heard, and no human heart has imagined. So understand. And this is general whether he explicitly stated that he gave fatwa based on opinion or not. And the denier said: secondly, if the opinion of a Companion were a proof, then two opposites would meet because some Companions contradict others in rulings, since there is difference in many issues. If you say: this is refuted by khabar al-wahid, for if it were a proof, two opposites would also meet due to contradiction in it. I say: there, one is abrogator of the other in reality, for the proof is one of them in reality, but due to our ignorance of history they conflict in our view, unlike our case, for there is no abrogation after his death (upon him and his family and companions be blessings and peace). The answer is that there is no contradiction here either, because hitting the truth was most often the case; when it fails, the truth is one of them in reality, but due to our ignorance of it and lack of priority, apparent conflict occurs, so no contradiction is necessary. Rather, the necessary thing is tarjih by opinion, or takhyir in action, or tawaquf, and one acts by qiyas or the principle according to difference of opinions, as will come. And the denier said: thirdly, if his opinion were a proof, it would necessitate taqlid.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute on whether the opinion of a Companion (qawl al-Sahabi) is binding evidence (hujja) for later mujtahids. It first lists scholars who hold it is hujja for non-Companions, including Hanafi authorities like al-Razi, al-Barda'i, al-Bazdawi, al-Sarakhsi, and also Malik, Shafi'i (old view), and Ahmad (one narration). The Hanafi position is that it is annexed to the Sunnah (mulhaq bi al-sunnah) meaning it must be followed. Shafi'i (new view) and Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi deny this. There is also a view that only the opinions of Abu Bakr and 'Umar are binding. The passage then limits the dispute to senior Companions, not later converts. It also notes that if a Companion's opinion contradicts widespread practice (amma al-balwa), it is not binding by agreement. The denier gives three arguments: (1) If it were hujja, any knowledgeable non-Companion's opinion would be hujja; the response is that the cause is the presumption of having heard from the Prophet and understanding through context, not mere knowledge. (2) Contradictions among Companions would lead to two opposites; the response distinguishes from khabar al-wahid because after the Prophet's death there is no abrogation, but truth is one in reality, and we may do tarjih or takhyir. (3) It would lead to taqlid; but this part is cut off. The overall Hanafi position is that the Companion's opinion carries weight due to their closeness to revelation, but it is not infallible.

Key Terms
قول الصحابي (qawl al-Sahabi): The legal opinion of a Companion of the Prophet (SAW) on a matter of ijtihad.
ملحق بالسنة (mulhaq bi al-sunnah): Annexed to the Sunnah, meaning it has the force of a proof that must be followed.
ظن السماع (zann al-sama'): The presumption that the Companion heard the ruling from the Prophet directly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the illah (cause) for making a Companion's opinion binding for non-Companions?
A) The Companion's infallibility (ismah).
B) The presumption that he heard the ruling from the Prophet and understood it correctly (zann al-sama').
C) The Companion's high social status or political authority.
D) The consensus of other Companions to follow that opinion.

Key Takeaway
For the Hanafis, qawl al-Sahabi is binding for non-Companions because of the strong likelihood it originates from the Prophet himself, based on their consistent reliance on texts.
